import torch
import torch.nn as nn
import torch.nn.functional as F
from einops import rearrange

class Model(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, batch_size, seq_length, n_heads, d_head, d_state, block_len=64):
        """
        Mamba Structured State Space model implementation for benchmarking.
        
        :param batch_size: Size of the batch
        :param seq_length: Length of the input sequence
        :param n_heads: Number of attention heads
        :param d_head: Dimension of each head
        :param d_state: Dimension of the state space
        :param block_len: Length of each block for chunked computation
        """
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        
        assert seq_length % block_len == 0, "Sequence length must be divisible by block length"
        
        self.batch_size = batch_size
        self.seq_length = seq_length
        self.n_heads = n_heads
        self.d_head = d_head
        self.d_state = d_state
        self.block_len = block_len
        
        # Initialize parameters
        self.A = nn.Parameter(torch.randn(batch_size, seq_length, n_heads))
        self.B = nn.Parameter(torch.randn(batch_size, seq_length, n_heads, d_state))
        self.C = nn.Parameter(torch.randn(batch_size, seq_length, n_heads, d_state))
        
    def segsum(self, x):
        """Naive segment sum calculation."""
        T = x.size(-1)
        x_cumsum = torch.cumsum(x, dim=-1)
        x_segsum = x_cumsum[..., :, None] - x_cumsum[..., None, :]
        mask = torch.tril(torch.ones(T, T, device=x.device, dtype=bool), diagonal=0)
        x_segsum = x_segsum.masked_fill(~mask, -torch.inf)
        return x_segsum
    
    def forward(self, X, initial_states=None):
        """
        Forward pass implementing the SSD operation.
        
        :param X: Input tensor of shape (batch, length, n_heads, d_head)
        :param initial_states: Optional initial states
        :return: Output tensor Y and final state
        """
        # Rearrange into blocks/chunks
        X_blocks, A_blocks, B_blocks, C_blocks = [
            rearrange(x, "b (c l) ... -> b c l ...", l=self.block_len)
            for x in (X, self.A, self.B, self.C)
        ]
        
        A_blocks = rearrange(A_blocks, "b c l h -> b h c l")
        A_cumsum = torch.cumsum(A_blocks, dim=-1)
        
        # 1. Compute diagonal block outputs
        L = torch.exp(self.segsum(A_blocks))
        Y_diag = torch.einsum("bclhn,bcshn,bhcls,bcshp->bclhp", 
                             C_blocks, B_blocks, L, X_blocks)
        
        # 2. Compute intra-chunk states
        decay_states = torch.exp((A_cumsum[:, :, :, -1:] - A_cumsum))
        states = torch.einsum("bclhn,bhcl,bclhp->bchpn", 
                            B_blocks, decay_states, X_blocks)
        
        # 3. Compute inter-chunk recurrence
        if initial_states is None:
            initial_states = torch.zeros_like(states[:, :1])
        states = torch.cat([initial_states, states], dim=1)
        
        decay_chunk = torch.exp(self.segsum(F.pad(A_cumsum[:, :, :, -1], (1, 0))))
        new_states = torch.einsum("bhzc,bchpn->bzhpn", decay_chunk, states)
        states = new_states[:, :-1]
        
        # 4. Compute state-to-output conversion
        state_decay_out = torch.exp(A_cumsum)
        Y_off = torch.einsum('bclhn,bchpn,bhcl->bclhp', 
                           C_blocks, states, state_decay_out)
        
        # Combine diagonal and off-diagonal terms
        Y = rearrange(Y_diag + Y_off, "b c l h p -> b (c l) h p")
        
        
        return Y

# Test parameters
batch_size = 2048
seq_length = 128
n_heads = 8
d_head = 64
d_state = 16
block_len = 64

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.rand(batch_size, seq_length, n_heads, d_head)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [batch_size, seq_length, n_heads, d_head, d_state, block_len]

reasoning
We need to write a custom CUDA kernel for the forward pass of a specific Mamba Structured State Space model (SSD). Let's restate the operations and shapes to understand the math. From the reference: Input X: (batch_size, seq_length, n_heads, d_head) Parameters: - A: (batch_size, seq_length, n_heads) - B: (batch_size, seq_length, n_heads, d_state) - C: (batch_size, seq_length, n_heads, d_state) Shapes: batch_size = 2048 seq_length = 128 n_heads = 8 d_head = 64 d_state = 16 block_len = 64 So number of chunks c = seq_length / block_len = 128 / 64 = 2. l = block_len = 64. h = n_heads = 8. p = d_head = 64. n = d_state = 16. b = batch_size = 2048. Rearrangements: X_blocks: (b, c, l, h, p) A_blocks: after rearrange, becomes (b, h, c, l). Wait: A was (b, seq_len, h). rearrange to "b c l ..." gives (b, c, l, h). Then A_blocks = rearrange(A_blocks, "b c l h -> b h c l"), so A_blocks: (b, h, c, l). B_blocks: (b, c, l, h, n) C_blocks: (b, c, l, h, n) A_cumsum = torch.cumsum(A_blocks, dim=-1) # along l: (b, h, c, l) 1. Diagonal block outputs: def segsum(x): x is shape (..., T) where T = l = 64. Actually segsum is applied to A_blocks which is (b, h, c, l). So segsum computes for each batch, head, chunk: a l x l lower-triangular matrix of segment sums. L = torch.exp(segsum(A_blocks)) # (b, h, c, l, l) Then Y_diag = einsum("bclhn,bcshn,bhcls,bcshp->bclhp", C_blocks, B_blocks, L, X_blocks) Let's decode indices: - C_blocks: (b, c, l, h, n) - B_blocks: (b, c, s (= l), h, n) (note s is also block length) - L: (b, h, c, l, s) - X_blocks: (b, c, s, h, p) The einsum does: For each b, c, l, h, p: Y_diag[b,c,l,h,p] = sum over s and n of C_blocks[b,c,l,h,n] * B_blocks[b,c,s,h,n] * L[b,h,c,l,s] * X_blocks[b,c,s,h,p] Wait, C and B share n, B and X don't share n, X shares p, L doesn't share n. Actually einsum string: "bclhn,bcshn,bhcls,bcshp->bclhp" The shared indices: - b: all - c: C, B, X (L has c) - l: C, L (first l) - h: all - n: C, B (so sum over n) - s: B, L, X (so sum over s) - p: X, output So Y_diag[b,c,l,h,p] = sum_{s,n} C[b,c,l,h,n] * B[b,c,s,h,n] * L[b,h,c,l,s] * X[b,c,s,h,p]. Yes. This is essentially for each chunk c, each head h, it's a block-local causal linear attention: Y_diag_{l,p} = sum_{s <= l} ( sum_n C_{l,n} B_{s,n} ) * exp( sum_{t=s}^{l} A_{t} ) * X_{s,p}. Since A is per-position per-head. L[b,h,c,l,s] = exp( sum_{t=s}^{l} A[b,h,c,t] ) for s <= l, else -inf -> 0. Why is there n? Actually C and B are low-rank state matrices (d_state=16). So the inner product over n yields a scalar. This is like a SSM scan within each chunk. 2. Intra-chunk states: decay_states = torch.exp( (A_cumsum[:, :, :, -1:] - A_cumsum) ) A_cumsum shape: (b, h, c, l). A_cumsum[..., -1:] shape: (b, h, c, 1). decay_states: (b, h, c, l). Wait: Let's compute broadcasting: A_cumsum[:,:,:,-1:] is (b,h,c,1). Subtract A_cumsum (b,h,c,l) -> (b,h,c,l). Then exp -> (b,h,c,l). So decay_states[b,h,c,l] = exp( A_cumsum_last - A_cumsum[b,h,c,l] ) = exp( - sum_{t=l+1}^{end} A_t ). This is the decay from position l to the end of the chunk. states = torch.einsum("bclhn,bhcl,bclhp->bchpn", B_blocks, decay_states, X_blocks) Wait: B_blocks is (b,c,l,h,n). decay_states is (b,h,c,l) which rearranges to (b,c,l,h) if we permute, but einsum string says "bhcl". So in the einsum: B has indices b,c,l,h,n; decay_states has b,h,c,l; X has b,c,l,h,p. Output: b,c,h,p,n. Let's check: sum over l (the inner block positions). So states[b,c,h,p,n] = sum_l B[b,c,l,h,n] * decay_states[b,h,c,l] * X[b,c,l,h,p]. This is like accumulating the effect of each position in the chunk into a state vector, weighed by decay to the end of the chunk. 3. Inter-chunk recurrence: if initial_states is None: initial_states = torch.zeros_like(states[:, :1]) states = torch.cat([initial_states, states], dim=1) Now states: (b, c+1, h, p, n). For c=2, it becomes (b,3,h,p,n). initial is (b,1,h,p,n), then concat with states (b,2,h,p,n). decay_chunk = torch.exp(self.segsum(F.pad(A_cumsum[:, :, :, -1], (1, 0)))) A_cumsum[:,:,:,-1] is (b,h,c). Pad with (1,0) along last dim -> (b,h,c+1). segsum applied to this: (b,h,c+1,c+1). So decay_chunk: (b,h,c+1,c+1). self.segsum(x) does for T = c+1: x_cumsum over T, then differences, mask lower-triangular. So decay_chunk[b,h,z,c] = exp( sum_{t=c}^{z} A_chunk_t ), where A_chunk_t = A_cumsum[b,h,t,-1] (the last cumsum in chunk t). This defines inter-chunk decay. new_states = torch.einsum("bhzc,bchpn->bzhpn", decay_chunk, states) Wait: decay_chunk is (b,h,z,c) where z and c are chunk indices. states is (b,c,h,p,n). Output (b,z,h,p,n). So for each output chunk z: new_states[b,z,h,p,n] = sum_c decay_chunk[b,h,z,c] * states[b,c,h,p,n] Because segsum masks upper-triangular? Actually mask = tril(..., diagonal=0). So for z >= c, it's valid, else -inf (0). So it's a causal sum over chunks c <= z of decay_chunk[b,h,z,c] * states[b,c,h,p,n]. Since states includes initial_states at c=0, this does a recurrence across chunks. Then states = new_states[:, :-1] # drop the last chunk index? Wait: new_states had length c+1 (z dimension). Then states takes all but the last: shape (b, c, h, p, n). So it's the state *after* processing each chunk, but before the last? Actually there are c chunks of input (c=2). states before cat was (b,c,h,p,n). After cat (b,c+1,h,p,n). After einsum new_states is (b,c+1,h,p,n). Then states = new_states[:, :-1] gives (b,c,h,p,n). So for each chunk index i (0..c-1), states[b,i] is the state after processing chunk i (including initial). This makes sense. 4. State-to-output conversion: state_decay_out = torch.exp(A_cumsum) # (b,h,c,l) Y_off = torch.einsum('bclhn,bchpn,bhcl->bclhp', C_blocks, states, state_decay_out) Let's decode: C: b,c,l,h,n; states: b,c,h,p,n; state_decay_out: b,h,c,l. Output: b,c,l,h,p. Sum over n and p? Wait: Y_off[b,c,l,h,p] = sum_n C[b,c,l,h,n] * states[b,c,h,p,n] * state_decay_out[b,h,c,l]. Wait, einsum string: 'bclhn,bchpn,bhcl->bclhp' Shared: - b - c - l (C and state_decay_out) - h (all) - n (C and states) - p (states and output) So Y_off[b,c,l,h,p] = sum_n ( C[b,c,l,h,n] * states[b,c,h,p,n] ) * state_decay_out[b,h,c,l]. Wait, where does p go? It's in states and output. The sum is over n only. So: Y_off[...,p] = sum_n C[...,n] * states[...,p,n] * state_decay_out[...]. This is applying the state (which is a matrix of shape (p,n)) to C (vector n) scaled by decay. Then Y = rearrange(Y_diag + Y_off, "b c l h p -> b (c l) h p") Return Y. So overall, we need to replicate this exactly. Given shapes: batch=2048, seq_len=128, n_heads=8, d_head=64, d_state=16, block_len=64. Number of chunks c = 2. l = 64. Because c=2 is small, some operations are tiny. But batch size is huge (2048). So a lot of parallelism across batch and heads. Let's analyze per-operation computational cost. 1. Rearranges: These are just view operations, no data movement if contiguous, but might involve permutes. We can fuse them into the kernel or let PyTorch handle them. However, A needs to be permuted to (b, h, c, l) for the kernel. We can do that in Python with permute or rearrange. 2. segsum(A_blocks): A_blocks shape (b, h, c, l). For each (b,h,c), compute l x l lower-triangular matrix of segment sums. Complexity: O(b * h * c * l^2) = 2048 * 8 * 2 * 64^2 = 2048 * 8 * 2 * 4096 = 134,217,728 operations (~134M). This is moderate. But wait, segsum uses cumsum and then subtraction and masking. It generates an l x l matrix. For l=64, l^2=4096. With b=2048, h=8, c=2: 2048*8*2=32768 slices. Each slice does a 64 cumsum (64 ops) and then a 64x64 difference matrix (4096 ops) and mask. Total ~134M. This is doable. However, we need to be careful about memory bandwidth. The l x l matrix L has size b*h*c*l*l = 2048*8*2*64*64 = 67,108,864 floats. At 4 bytes each, that's ~256 MB. That's large but fits in GPU memory. But reading/writing it will take time. The subsequent einsum uses L. Since l=64, c=2, maybe fusing segsum with the einsum is beneficial. 3. Y_diag einsum: It involves C, B, L, X. For each (b,c,l,h,p), sum over s and n. n=16, s=64. So inner loop is 16*64 = 1024 multiplies and adds. Number of output elements: b*c*l*h*p = 2048*2*64*8*64 = 67,108,864. Total FLOPs: ~68.7B FLOPs. That's large! Actually 67M outputs * 1024 ops = ~68.7B multiply-adds. That's substantial. For fp32, at ~20 TFLOPS, that's ~3.4 ms. For A100 maybe 1-2 ms. DRAM bandwidth might be lower if loading L (256 MB), C (2048*128*8*16*4 = 67M? Let's compute: b*seq_len*n_heads*d_state = 2048*128*8*16 = 33,554,432 elements * 4 bytes = ~128 MB). B same size. X: b*seq_len*n_heads*d_head = 2048*128*8*64 = 134,217,728 elements * 4 = 512 MB. So total reads for Y_diag: ~128+128+512 + 256 (L) = ~1 GB. Write: Y_diag is same size as X: 512 MB. So memory traffic ~1.5 GB. At 1.5 TB/s, that's ~1 ms. But compute is 68.7B FLOPs, at 10 TFLOPS (conservative) that's 6.9 ms. So compute bound. But wait, is L reused across p? L doesn't depend on p. So if we compute Y_diag across p in tiles, we can keep L in shared memory. Similarly C and B are independent of p, X depends on p. So this is a matrix multiplication-like operation. We can formulate it as: Let M[b,c,l,s,h] = sum_n C[b,c,l,h,n] * B[b,c,s,h,n] * L[b,h,c,l,s] Then Y_diag[b,c,l,h,p] = sum_s M[b,c,l,s,h] * X[b,c,s,h,p]. For each (b,c,h), M is l x l. X is l x p. Y is l x p. So it's batched matrix multiply: Y = M @ X, where M is lower triangular (causal). M = (C @ B^T) * L. C: (l, n), B: (l, n). C @ B^T -> (l, l). Then pointwise multiply with L (l,l). Then M @ X -> (l, p). For each chunk c and head h and batch b. Total number of such batched GEMMs: b*c*h = 2048*2*8 = 32768. Each is (64 x 16) * (16 x 64) to get (64x64) for M, then (64x64) * (64x64) to get Y. Well, X is l x p = 64 x 64. So M is 64x64, X is 64x64. Y is 64x64. So per (b,c,h): - Compute CB^T: 64x16 * 16x64 = 64k FLOPs (actually 64*16*64*2 = 131k? multiply-add). - Pointwise mul with L: 64*64 = 4k FLOPs. - M @ X: 64x64 * 64x64 = 64*64*64*2 = 524k FLOPs. Total ~660k FLOPs per (b,c,h). Total FLOPs: 32768 * 660k = 21.6B FLOPs. Wait, earlier I counted 68.7B. Let's recalc: outputs are b*c*l*h*p = 2048*2*64*8*64 = 67,108,864 elements. Each element is sum over s (64) and n (16). So naive FLOPs = outputs * (s*n) multiplications + outputs * (s*n-1) additions ~ 67M * 1024 ~ 68.7B. But if we rewrite as (C B^T) * L then matmul with X, the FLOPs are different: - C B^T: for each (b,c,h), C is l x n = 64x16, B^T is n x l = 16x64. That's a matmul of size (64,16)*(16,64) -> (64,64). FLOPs = 2*64*16*64 = 131,072 per (b,c,h). Total = 32768 * 131072 = 4.29B. - M @ X: (64,64)*(64,64) -> (64,64). FLOPs = 2*64*64*64 = 524,288 per (b,c,h). Total = 32768 * 524288 = 17.18B. Total = 21.47B. This is 3x less FLOPs than the naive einsum because the einsum was not optimized (PyTorch might optimize it?). Actually PyTorch's einsum might optimize it with contraction path? Let's see: einsum("bclhn,bcshn,bhcls,bcshp->bclhp"). PyTorch's einsum uses opt_einsum? I think PyTorch's einsum greedily contracts. It might contract C and B first over n -> (b,c,l,s,h). Then multiply with L -> still (b,c,l,s,h). Then contract with X over s -> (b,c,l,h,p). So it would do the same two matmuls. So FLOPs are ~21.5B. But still, for fp32 on an A100 (19.5 TFLOPS for FP32 Tensor Core? Actually A100 80GB has 19.5 TFLOPS FP32 dense, 312 TFLOPS FP16/TF32). But we are doing FP32. So 21.5B FLOPs at 19 TFLOPS is ~1.1 ms. Memory: L is 256MB, C/B/X are 128+128+512=768MB. Y_diag is 512MB. So total reads/writes ~1.5GB. At 2TB/s, ~0.75ms. So it's likely compute bound or mixed. A custom kernel that fuses everything (computing L on the fly, doing CB^T, pointwise mul, then M@X) could be efficient. However, we also have Y_off and the states computation. Let's compute their costs. Intra-chunk states: states = einsum("bclhn,bhcl,bclhp->bchpn", B_blocks, decay_states, X_blocks) This is sum over l: B[b,c,l,h,n] * decay_states[b,h,c,l] * X[b,c,l,h,p]. For each (b,c,h), we need to compute a matrix of size (p, n) = (64, 16). We can think of it as: for each l, outer product of X[b,c,l,h,:] (p) and B[b,c,l,h,:] (n), scaled by decay_states. Then sum over l. So it's like X^T @ diag(decay) @ B. X is (l,p), B is (l,n). decay is (l). So states per (b,c,h) = X^T @ (B * decay[:,None])? Wait: sum_l X_{p} * (B_{n} * decay). Actually it's sum_l (decay_l * X_{l,p}) * B_{l,n}? Let's check indices: einsum "bclhn,bhcl,bclhp->bchpn". So sum over l: B_{l,n} * decay_l * X_{l,p}. So yes, it's X^T @ (B * decay[:, None])? No, X is (l,p), B is (l,n). So (X^T) is (p,l). (B * decay[:,None]) is (l,n). So (X^T) @ (B * decay[:,None]) is (p,n). But the order is actually sum_l X_{l,p} * (B_{l,n} * decay_l). That equals X^T @ (B * decay[:,None]) because (X^T)_{p,l} = X_{l,p}. So it's a matmul: (p,l) @ (l,n) -> (p,n). FLOPs per (b,c,h): 2 * p * l * n = 2 * 64 * 64 * 16 = 131,072. Total: 32768 * 131072 = 4.29B FLOPs. Size of states: b*c*h*p*n = 2048*2*8*64*16 = 33,554,432 elements = 128 MB. Inter-chunk recurrence: decay_chunk = exp(segsum(pad(A_cumsum[:,:,:,-1], (1,0)))) A_cumsum_last: (b,h,c). Pad to (b,h,c+1). c=2 -> c+1=3. segsum over 3 elements: trivial. decay_chunk: (b,h,c+1,c+1) = (2048,8,3,3). Very small. 147k elements. einsum("bhzc,bchpn->bzhpn", decay_chunk, states): For each (b,h,z,p,n) sum over c. c=2 (actually c+1=3 input states, output z=c+1=3, then drop last -> 2). So it's essentially a small causal matmul across chunks. For each (b,h,p,n), we have a vector of length 3 (states across chunks plus initial), and a 3x3 lower-triangular matrix decay_chunk. Output length 3. FLOPs: b*h*(c+1)^2*p*n = 2048*8*9*64*16 = ~151M. Very small. Memory: states is 128MB, decay_chunk tiny. Then states = new_states[:, :-1] -> (b,c,h,p,n) = 128MB. State-to-output conversion: Y_off = einsum('bclhn,bchpn,bhcl->bclhp', C_blocks, states, state_decay_out) = sum_n C_{l,n} * states_{p,n} * state_decay_out_l. For each (b,c,h,l,p): Y_off = state_decay_out_l * sum_n C_{l,n} * states_{p,n}. So for each (b,c,h), we have C: (l,n), states: (p,n). Compute (C @ states^T): (l,p). Then scale each row l by state_decay_out_l. FLOPs per (b,c,h): 2 * l * n * p = 2*64*16*64 = 131,072. Total: 32768 * 131072 = 4.29B FLOPs. Memory: C is 128MB, states is 128MB, state_decay_out is A_cumsum size: b*h*c*l = 2048*8*2*64 = 2,097,152 = 8MB. Y_off is 512MB. So overall: - Y_diag: ~21.5B FLOPs (dominant) - States (intra): ~4.3B FLOPs - Inter-chunk: ~0.15B FLOPs - Y_off: ~4.3B FLOPs Total FLOPs ~30B. A PyTorch reference might be spending a lot of time in intermediate allocations, permutations, and einsum dispatch overhead. Also it computes segsum, which creates a large L tensor, and does multiple separate kernels. A custom CUDA kernel can fuse everything into one or a few kernels to minimize memory traffic and launch overhead. Our goal: Write a custom CUDA kernel that does the whole forward pass efficiently. Given that c=2, l=64, we can consider a kernel per operation or a single fused kernel. Because c=2, the block dimension along c is 2. We could assign each block to process one chunk (c) or each thread block to process a (batch, head) pair for both chunks. Since c is small (2), we can process both chunks in one thread block if desired, but maybe better to have each thread block handle one chunk to get more parallelism: b*h*c = 2048*8*2 = 32768. That's plenty of blocks. Each block processes a (b, h, c) slice. For each (b, h, c), the data sizes are: - X: l x p = 64 * 64 = 4096 floats - B: l x n = 64 * 16 = 1024 floats - C: l x n = 64 * 16 = 1024 floats - A: l floats Total per slice: ~6k floats = 24KB. This fits nicely in shared memory. In fact, we could load A, B, C, X into shared memory for the slice and compute everything in registers. Given that l=64, n=16, p=64, we can design a thread block that computes: 1. A_cumsum (exclusive or inclusive) for the chunk. 2. L (causal matrix) for the chunk: L[l,s] = exp( sum_{t=s}^{l} A_t ). But we don't need to store full L if we compute Y_diag differently? Well, Y_diag = M @ X where M = (C @ B^T) * L. Since L is lower triangular and depends on A. 3. states_intra = X^T @ (B * decay) where decay_l = exp(A_last - A_cumsum_l). Actually decay_states_l = exp(A_cumsum_last - A_cumsum_l). 4. Very fast. Compute L on the fly: For a given (i,j), L = (j <= i) ? exp(prefix[i] - (j>0?prefix[j-1]:0)) : 0. Then M[i][j] = D[i][j] * L[i][j]. Store M in shared memory (or reuse D memory). But 60KB is fine. **Kernel 2: `ssd_inter_chunk_scan`** Inputs: intra_state (b, h, c, p, n), A_last (b, h, c). Output: chunk_states (b, h, c, p, n) (these are the states for each chunk to use in Y_off). Since c is small, we can compute the recurrence across c for each (b,h). We have c chunks. A_chunk = [0] + A_last (length c+1). We need decay_chunk = exp(segsum(A_chunk)). segsum on length c+1. Then new_states_c = sum_{k=0}^{c} decay_chunk[c,k] * intra_state_k (with intra_state_(-1) = initial_state = 0). Then chunk_states = new_states_{0..c-1}. Since c is small (e.g., 2), we can compute this in a small kernel. One block per (b,h). Each block has p*n = 1024 elements of state. Block size: we can use 256 or 512 threads. Each thread handles a few elements of the state matrix (p,n). We can compute A_chunk prefix in shared memory (c+1 floats). Then compute decay_chunk ( (c+1)*(c+1) floats ) in shared memory. Then apply to each state element independently across c. For each element index e (0..p*n-1), we have a vector intra_state_e[0..c-1] (length c). Let's design the kernel step by step. **Kernel signature:** ```cpp __global__ void ssd_forward( const float* __restrict__ A, const float* __restrict__ B, const float* __restrict__ C, const float* __restrict__ X, float* __restrict__ Y, int batch, int n_heads, int seq_len, int d_state, int d_head, int block_len) ``` Layout: A: (batch, n_heads, seq_len) B: (batch, n_heads, seq_len, d_state) C: (batch, n_heads, seq_len, d_state) X: (batch, n_heads, seq_len, d_head) Y: (batch, n_heads, seq_len, d_head) strides (since contiguous): A stride_h = seq_len A stride_b = n_heads * seq_len B stride_h = seq_len * d_state B stride_b = n_heads * seq_len * d_state C same as B. X stride_h = seq_len * d_head X stride_b = n_heads * seq_len * d_head Y same as X. Chunk offset: chunk_id * block_len. **Block mapping:** We can use a 2D grid: gridDim.x = batch, gridDim.y = n_heads. Or 1D: block_id = blockIdx.x; b = block_id / n_heads; h = block_id % n_heads. Let's use 1D grid of size batch * n_heads. Each block handles one (b,h) pair. Threads per block: let's use 256 threads (one dimension). Or maybe 128. 256 is good. **Pass 1: Loop over chunks** For chunk = 0 .. num_chunks-1: 1. Load A chunk: offset = ((b * n_heads + h) * seq_len + chunk * block_len). Load l elements into shared memory `smem_A`. - 256 threads, each loads l/256 = 64/256 = 0.25 elements? That's too few. Better to have each thread load multiple elements if needed. Actually we can have each thread load 1 element, but l=64. So 64 threads load one element each, the rest idle. That's fine. For simplicity, we can have threads with tid < l load A[tid]. Or we can loop with stride blockDim.x. Let's generalize: for (int i = tid; i < l; i += blockDim.x) smem_A[i] = A[...]. This works for any block size. 2. Load B, C, X similarly. B: size l*n = 1024. Each thread loads 4 elements. C: size 1024. X: size l*p = 4096. Each thread loads 16 elements. 3. __syncthreads(); 4. Compute prefix = cumsum(A) in shared memory. - Simple parallel prefix sum? l=64 is tiny. We can do it sequentially with 1 thread. Or collaborative. Since l=64, 1 thread can do it in 64 steps. But we have many blocks. Let's just have thread 0 do it: ``` if (tid == 0) { smem_prefix[0] = smem_A[0]; for (int i=1; i<l; i++) smem_prefix[i] = smem_prefix[i-1] + smem_A[i]; } __syncthreads(); ``` This is fine. It's only 64 adds per block. No need for parallel scan. 5. Compute A_last = smem_prefix[l-1]. Store in a register array `A_last_all[chunk]`. 6. Compute state_decay_out: for i = tid to l-1: smem_sdo[i] = expf(smem_prefix[i]). Also compute decay_later: decay[i] = expf(A_last - smem_prefix[i]). 7. Compute D = C @ B^T (l x l). We need to compute for each (i,j): sum_n C[i,n] * B[j,n]. Let's assign each thread a set of (i,j) pairs. total_pairs = l * l = 4096. blockDim.x = 256. So each thread handles 4096/256 = 16 pairs. We can loop: ``` for (int idx = tid; idx < l*l; idx += blockDim.x) { int i = idx / l; int j = idx % l; float sum = 0; for (int n_id = 0; n_id < d_state; n_id++) { sum += smem_C[i*d_state + n_id] * smem_B[j*d_state + n_id]; } // compute L and M directly float val = 0.0f; if (j <= i) { float pref_i = smem_prefix[i]; float pref_jm1 = (j > 0) ? smem_prefix[j-1] : 0.0f; val = expf(pref_i - pref_jm1); } smem_D[idx] = sum * val; // M } ``` Wait, but we also need D for something else? No, only M = D * L. So we can compute directly into M (reuse smem_D as M). This loop does 16 * 16 = 256 multiplies and 15*16 adds per thread. That's 256 FMAs per thread. Fast. Note: `smem_B[j*d_state + n_id]` - we need to ensure B is stored as (l, n) in smem. Yes. C is also (l, n). 8. __syncthreads(); 9. Compute Y_diag: M @ X -> (l,p). Output size l*p = 4096. Each thread handles some output elements. For idx = tid; idx < l*p; idx += blockDim.x: i = idx / p; p_idx = idx % p; float sum = 0; for (int j=0; j<l; j++) { sum += smem_D[i*l + j] * smem_X[j*p + p_idx]; } Y_diag_all[chunk*l*p + idx] = sum; Note: smem_D is M (l x l). But note M is lower triangular. However, the inner loop still goes over all j, but for j>i, M=0, so it adds zero. This is fine. Each thread does 16 * 64 = 1024 FMAs. Good. 10. __syncthreads(); 11. Compute intra_state: X^T @ (B * decay) -> (p,n). First, we can compute B_decayed = B[j,n] * decay[j] and store in smem_B (overwriting B). Or just multiply on the fly. Since we no longer need original B after this, we can reuse smem_B. For idx = tid; idx < l*n; idx += blockDim.x: j = idx / n; n_id = idx % n; smem_B[idx] *= decay[j]; __syncthreads(); Then compute intra_state: total_elements = p * n = 1024. For idx = tid; idx < p*n; idx += blockDim.x: p_idx = idx / n; n_id = idx % n; float sum = 0; for (int j=0; j<l; j++) { sum += smem_X[j*p + p_idx] * smem_B[j*n + n_id]; } intra_all[chunk*p*n + idx] = sum; But wait: X^T @ B_decayed uses X[j,p_idx] and B_decayed[j,n_id]. Yes. Each thread does 1024/256 = 4 elements; each element loops l=64. So 256 FMAs per thread. 12. __syncthreads(); 13. Save state_decay_out for later? We need it for Y_off. We can save it in a persistent shared memory area: state_decay_out_all[chunk*l + i]. Or recompute it during Pass 2. Recomputing requires loading A and doing prefix sum again. That's expensive. Better save it. Let's allocate shared memory for state_decay_out_all: c * l floats. For i = tid; i < l; i += blockDim.x: sdo_all[chunk*l + i] = expf(smem_prefix[i]); 14. End of chunk loop. **Pass 2: Inter-chunk scan** After the loop, we have: - A_last_all[c] - intra_all[c][p][n] - Y_diag_all[c][l][p] - sdo_all[c][l] Compute chunk_states (size c * p * n). We can compute them in place in a new array `state_all[c][p][n]`. Since c is small, we can compute the scan with one thread or collaboratively. Let's have thread 0 do it: ``` // Build decay_chunk matrix (c+1 x c+1) // We don't need full matrix. For each output chunk i (0..c-1): // state_i = sum_{k=0}^{i-1} intra_k * exp( sum_{t=k+1}^{i} A_last_t ) // Where A_last_t is the total A for chunk t. // The product of chunk decays: decay_factor[k][i] = exp( sum_{t=k+1}^{i} A_last_t ). // With c=2: state_0=0, state_1 = intra_0 * exp(A_last_0). // Let's compute prefix of A_last. float chunk_prefix[8]; // assuming c <= 8 or something. chunk_prefix[0] = A_last_all[0]; for (int i=1; i<c-1; i++) chunk_prefix[i] = chunk_prefix[i-1] + A_last_all[i]; // Actually we need sum from k+1 to i. // For each i, state_i = sum_{k=0}^{i-1} intra_all[k] * exp( chunk_prefix[i-1] - (k>0?chunk_prefix[k-1]:0) ); // But c might be larger. Let's generic loop. Since c is small and per element, we can do for each element index. ``` Actually, since the state is a matrix (p,n) per chunk, and c is small, we can compute chunk_states independently for each element index. So we can parallelize over p*n elements. Specifically, we can have each thread handle a few indices e in [0, p*n-1]. For each e, we have a vector `intra_vec[k] = intra_all[k][e]` for k=0..c-1. We want `state_vec[i]` for i=0..c-1: `state_vec[i] = sum_{k=0}^{i-1} intra_vec[k] * exp( sum_{t=k+1}^{i} A_last_all[t] )`. Note: intra_vec is not contiguous across k in memory if intra_all is stored as c x p x n. But we can index it. Since c is small (e.g., 2), each thread does a tiny loop. Let's compute `chunk_A_prefix` (exclusive) of A_last_all. `chunk_A_prefix[0] = 0; chunk_A_prefix[i] = chunk_A_prefix[i-1] + A_last_all[i-1];` for i=1..c. Then sum_{t=k+1}^{i} A_last_all[t] = chunk_A_prefix[i+1] - chunk_A_prefix[k+1]? Let's check. A_last_all indices 0..c-1. sum_{t=k+1}^{i} A_last_all[t] = (sum_{t=0}^{i} A_last_all[t]) - (sum_{t=0}^{k} A_last_all[t]). Let prefix_A[i] = sum_{t=0}^{i-1} A_last_all[t], with prefix_A[0]=0, prefix_A[1]=A_last_all[0], prefix_A[2]=A_last_all[0]+A_last_all[1], etc. length c+1. Then sum_{t=k+1}^{i} = prefix_A[i+1] - prefix_A[k+1]. So decay = exp(prefix_A[i+1] - prefix_A[k+1]). Then state_i = sum_{k=0}^{i-1} intra_all[k] * exp(prefix_A[i+1] - prefix_A[k+1]). This is exactly a causal linear scan. Since c is small, we can compute prefix_A in a shared array of size c+1. Then loop. Implementation for each thread handling element e: ``` float prefix_A[8]; // assume c <= max_c prefix_A[0] = 0; for (int i=0; i<c; i++) prefix_A[i+1] = prefix_A[i] + A_last_all[i]; for (int idx = tid; idx < p*n; idx += blockDim.x) { // compute state for each chunk i for (int i=0; i<c; i++) { float sum = 0.0f; for (int k=0; k<i; k++) { float decay = expf(prefix_A[i+1] - prefix_A[k+1]); sum += intra_all[k * (p*n) + idx] * decay; } state_all[i * (p*n) + idx] = sum; } } ``` For c=2, each thread does: - i=0: sum=0. - i=1: sum = intra_all[0] * exp(prefix_A[1] - prefix_A[1]) = intra_all[0] * 1? Wait: prefix_A[1] = A_last_0. prefix_A[k+1] for k=0 is prefix_A[1] = A_last_0. decay = exp(A_last_0 - A_last_0) = 1. So state_1 = intra_all[0]. But earlier we derived state_1 = intra_all[0] * exp(A_last_0). What's wrong? Let's re-derive carefully. Reference: states_cat = [initial_states, states_0, states_1, ..., states_{c-1}]. A_cumsum_last = [A_last_0, A_last_1, ...]. Pad(A_cumsum_last, (1,0)) -> [0, A_last_0, A_last_1, ...]. segsum on this vector of length c+1: x_segsum[z, k] = sum_{t=k}^{z} padded[t]. decay_chunk[z,k] = exp(x_segsum[z,k]). padded[0] = 0, padded[i+1] = A_last_i. For z=i (output chunk index, 0-indexed from original c chunks): Wait, new_states has dimension c+1 (z=0..c). Then states = new_states[:, :-1] (z=0..c-1). For a given output chunk index z (0 <= z < c): state_z = new_states_z = sum_{k=0}^{c} decay_chunk[z, k] * states_cat_k. states_cat_k: k=0 is initial_state (0). k=1..c is intra_{k-1}. So state_z = sum_{k=1}^{c} decay_chunk[z, k] * intra_{k-1}. Let m = k-1. Then state_z = sum_{m=0}^{c-1} decay_chunk[z, m+1] * intra_m. But because of causal mask (lower triangular), decay_chunk[z,k] is zero for k > z. So the sum is actually m=0..z-1? Wait: k <= z for non-zero. Since k = m+1, m+1 <= z => m <= z-1. So: state_z = sum_{m=0}^{z-1} decay_chunk[z, m+1] * intra_m. Now decay_chunk[z, m+1] = exp( sum_{t=m+1}^{z} padded[t] ). padded[t] for t >= 1 is A_last_{t-1}. So sum_{t=m+1}^{z} padded[t] = sum_{t=m+1}^{z} A_last_{t-1} = sum_{j=m}^{z-1} A_last_j. Let prefix_A[i] = sum_{j=0}^{i-1} A_last_j. Then sum_{j=m}^{z-1} A_last_j = prefix_A[z] - prefix_A[m]. So decay_chunk[z, m+1] = exp(prefix_A[z] - prefix_A[m]). Thus: state_z = sum_{m=0}^{z-1} intra_m * exp(prefix_A[z] - prefix_A[m]). Let's check for z=0: sum empty -> 0. Correct. For z=1: state_1 = intra_0 * exp(prefix_A[1] - prefix_A[0]) = intra_0 * exp(A_last_0). That matches earlier derivation: Y_off for chunk 1 uses intra_0 scaled by exp(A_last_0). So my previous formula with prefix_A[i+1] - prefix_A[k+1] was off by one. The correct one uses prefix_A[z] - prefix_A[m]. Let's recompute the prefix: prefix_A[0] = 0. prefix_A[i] = sum_{t=0}^{i-1} A_last_t for i=1..c. Then state_z = sum_{m=0}^{z-1} intra_m * exp(prefix_A[z] - prefix_A[m]). We can compute this using a sequential loop for each z. Since c is small, for each element we can do: ``` float acc = 0.0f; for (int z=0; z<c; ++z) { // We want state_z. As we iterate z, we can maintain a running sum. // But the decay factor for each m changes with z. // However, we can compute prefix_A first. } ``` Since c is small, nested loops are fine. Let's compute prefix_A in shared memory (size c+1). Thread 0 does it. ``` if (tid == 0) { prefix_A[0] = 0; for (int i=0; i<c; ++i) prefix_A[i+1] = prefix_A[i] + A_last_all[i]; } __syncthreads(); ``` Note: we need prefix_A up to c. prefix_A[c] = sum of all A_last. But state_z uses prefix_A[z] for z=0..c-1. Then for each thread and each element index e: ``` for (int e_idx = tid; e_idx < p*n; e_idx += blockDim.x) { // Gather intra vector for this element // Since c is small, we can read intra_m on the fly inside loops. for (int z=0; z<c; ++z) { float sum = 0.0f; for (int m=0; m<z; ++m) { float intra_m = intra_all[m * (p*n) + e_idx]; float decay = expf(prefix_A[z] - prefix_A[m]); sum += intra_m * decay; } state_all[z * (p*n) + e_idx] = sum; } } ``` For c=2: each thread loops over e (4 elements) and for each e, z=0 (0 ops) and z=1 (1 multiply). So very cheap. For general c, the inner loop is O(c^2). With c maybe up to 16 or 32, and 16384 blocks each doing p*n=1024 elements, total work is 16384 * 1024 * c^2. If c=32, that's 16k * 1k * 1024 = 16B ops. But c=32 means l=4 (if seq_len=128) or seq_len=2048 l=64 => c=32. That could be heavy if done naively. But we can optimize the scan using a prefix product approach. Actually, state_z is a standard recurrence: state_0 = 0. For z = 1..c-1: state_z = intra_{z-1} + exp(A_last_{z-1}) * state_{z-1}? Let's check: state_z = sum_{m=0}^{z-1} intra_m * exp(prefix_A[z] - prefix_A[m]). state_{z-1} = sum_{m=0}^{z-2} intra_m * exp(prefix_A[z-1] - prefix_A[m]). So state_z = intra_{z-1} * exp(prefix_A[z] - prefix_A[z-1]) + sum_{m=0}^{z-2} intra_m * exp(prefix_A[z] - prefix_A[m]) = intra_{z-1} * exp(A_last_{z-1}) + exp(A_last_{z-1}) * sum_{m=0}^{z-2} intra_m * exp(prefix_A[z-1] - prefix_A[m]) = exp(A_last_{z-1}) * ( intra_{z-1} + state_{z-1} ). Wait, is that correct? Let's verify for z=1: state_1 = exp(A_last_0) * (intra_0 + state_0) = exp(A_last_0) * intra_0. Matches. For z=2: state_2 = exp(A_last_1) * (intra_1 + state_1) = exp(A_last_1) * intra_1 + exp(A_last_1 + A_last_0) * intra_0. From formula: sum_{m=0}^{1} intra_m * exp(prefix_A[2] - prefix_A[m]). = intra_0 * exp(A_last_0 + A_last_1) + intra_1 * exp(A_last_1). Matches! So the recurrence is simply: ``` state_0 = 0 for z=1..c-1: state_z = expf(A_last_{z-1}) * (intra_{z-1} + state_{z-1}) ``` Wait, does this hold for all z? Let's check derivation: prefix_A[z] - prefix_A[m] = (prefix_A[z] - prefix_A[z-1]) + (prefix_A[z-1] - prefix_A[m]) = A_last_{z-1} + (prefix_A[z-1] - prefix_A[m]). So exp(prefix_A[z] - prefix_A[m]) = exp(A_last_{z-1}) * exp(prefix_A[z-1] - prefix_A[m]). Thus: state_z = sum_{m=0}^{z-1} intra_m * exp(A_last_{z-1}) * exp(prefix_A[z-1] - prefix_A[m]) = exp(A_last_{z-1}) * ( intra_{z-1} * exp(prefix_A[z-1] - prefix_A[z-1]) + sum_{m=0}^{z-2} intra_m * exp(prefix_A[z-1] - prefix_A[m]) ) = exp(A_last_{z-1}) * ( intra_{z-1} + state_{z-1} ). Since exp(prefix_A[z-1] - prefix_A[z-1]) = exp(0) = 1. Yes! This is a simple first-order recurrence. This is a huge simplification! We don't need a matrix multiply or any complex scan. We just do: Initialize current_state = 0 (matrix of zeros). For chunk z = 0 .. c-1: // Before processing chunk z, current_state is state_z (the state from previous chunks). // Then we compute Y_off for chunk z using current_state. // After processing chunk z, update current_state = exp(A_last_z) * (intra_z + current_state). // This current_state becomes state_{z+1} for the next chunk. Wait, let's align indices: For chunk 0: state_0 = 0. Y_off_0 uses state_0 = 0. After chunk 0: next_state = exp(A_last_0) * (intra_0 + state_0) = exp(A_last_0) * intra_0. This is state_1. For chunk 1: Y_off_1 uses state_1 = next_state. So we can compute Y_off in the same loop as we compute intra_state, if we maintain the state across chunks. This means we might not need a separate pass at all! We can do the whole thing in a single loop over chunks per (b,h). Let's redesign the fused kernel: Each block (b,h): - current_state (p,n) initialized to 0 (in registers or shared memory). Since p*n=1024, and we have 256 threads, each thread can hold 4 floats of current_state in registers. - Loop chunk = 0 .. c-1: 1. Load A, B, C, X for the chunk. 2. Compute prefix, A_last. 3. Compute Y_diag (as before). 4. Compute intra_state (as before). 5. Compute Y_off: Y_off[i][p] = exp(prefix[i]) * sum_n C[i][n] * current_state[p][n]. Wait, current_state is (p,n). So sum_n C[i][n] * current_state[p][n] = (C @ current_state^T)[i][p]. Then scale each row i by exp(prefix[i]). We have C in shared memory (l,n). We have current_state in shared memory or registers. Let's store current_state in shared memory for easy access: size p*n = 1024 floats = 4KB. Then: - Compute Y_off into a temporary shared memory or directly accumulate. Actually Y_off = state_decay_out[:,None] * (C @ current_state^T). We can compute it as: For idx = tid; idx < l*p; idx += blockDim.x: i = idx / p; p_idx = idx % p; float sum = 0; for (int n_id=0; n_id < d_state; n_id++) { sum += smem_C[i*d_state + n_id] * current_state[p_idx*d_state + n_id]; } float val = sum * expf(smem_prefix[i]); Y_diag_all[chunk*l*p + idx] += val; // accumulate into Y_diag buffer Wait, Y_diag_all currently holds Y_diag. We can add Y_off to it in place. 6. Update current_state = expf(A_last) * (intra_state + current_state). Here intra_state is a (p,n) matrix. For each element e: current_state[e] = expf(A_last) * (intra_state[e] + current_state[e]); We can do this in shared memory. 7. Write final Y for this chunk to global memory: For idx = tid; idx < l*p; idx += blockDim.x): Y[base + idx] = Y_diag_all[chunk*l*p + idx]; 8. __syncthreads(); End loop. This is extremely efficient! Now, we just need to implement this in a single kernel per (b,h). The number of blocks is batch * n_heads = 16384. Each block processes c chunks sequentially. Shared memory layout: We need: - A: l - prefix: l - B: l*n - C: l*n - X: l*p - D/M: l*l - Y_diag: l*p (to accumulate before writing? Actually we can compute Y_diag into registers or shared memory then add Y_off.) - current_state: p*n - intra_state: p*n Let's see if we can fit everything. l=64, n=16, p=64. A: 64 * 4 = 256 B prefix: 256 B B: 64*16*4 = 4096 B C: 4096 B X: 64*64*4 = 16384 B M: 64*64*4 = 16384 B Y_diag: 64*64*4 = 16384 B current_state: 64*16*4 = 4096 B intra_state: 64*16*4 = 4096 B Total = 256+256+4096+4096+16384+16384+16384+4096+4096 = 56,832 B = 55.5 KB. That's very comfortable. Actually, we can reuse some arrays to save memory. For example, after computing Y_diag, we don't need M anymore. But they might be used concurrently. Let's design the shared memory partitions. ``` extern __shared__ float smem[]; // Partition: float* smem_A = smem; // size l float* smem_prefix = smem_A + l; // size l float* smem_B = smem_prefix + l; // size l*n float* smem_C = smem_B + l*n; // size l*n float* smem_X = smem_C + l*n; // size l*p float* smem_M = smem_X + l*p; // size l*l float* smem_Y_diag = smem_M + l*l; // size l*p float* smem_intra = smem_Y_diag + l*p; // size p*n float* smem_state = smem_intra + p*n; // size p*n ``` Total size = l*2 + l*n*2 + l*p*2 + l*l + p*n*2. = 128 + 2048 + 8192 + 4096 + 2048 = 16512 floats = 66KB. Good. But we need to ensure that `blockDim.x` is enough to load data and perform reductions efficiently. Let's set `blockDim.x = 256` (threads per block). **Loading data:** For each chunk, base pointers: ``` int b = blockIdx.x / n_heads; int h = blockIdx.x % n_heads; int chunk_offset = chunk * block_len; int base_A = ((b * n_heads + h) * seq_len + chunk_offset); int base_B = ((b * n_heads + h) * seq_len + chunk_offset) * d_state; int base_C = base_B; int base_X = ((b * n_heads + h) * seq_len + chunk_offset) * d_head; int base_Y = base_X; // Y has same layout as X ``` But careful: X layout is (b, h, seq_len, d_head). So index = ((b * n_heads + h) * seq_len + t) * d_head + p_idx. So base_X = ((b * n_heads + h) * seq_len + chunk_offset) * d_head. Similarly base_B = ((b * n_heads + h) * seq_len + chunk_offset) * d_state. base_A = ((b * n_heads + h) * seq_len + chunk_offset). Loading loops: ``` for (int i = tid; i < l; i += blockDim.x) smem_A[i] = A[base_A + i]; for (int i = tid; i < l*n; i += blockDim.x) smem_B[i] = B[base_B + i]; for (int i = tid; i < l*n; i += blockDim.x) smem_C[i] = C[base_C + i]; for (int i = tid; i < l*p; i += blockDim.x) smem_X[i] = X[base_X + i]; ``` __syncthreads(); **Step: Compute prefix** ``` if (tid == 0) { smem_prefix[0] = smem_A[0]; for (int i=1; i<l; i++) smem_prefix[i] = smem_prefix[i-1] + smem_A[i]; } __syncthreads(); ``` **Step: Compute M (l x l)** ``` for (int idx = tid; idx < l*l; idx += blockDim.x) { int i = idx / l; int j = idx % l; float sum = 0.0f; // dot product over n #pragma unroll for (int n_id = 0; n_id < d_state; n_id++) { sum += smem_C[i * d_state + n_id] * smem_B[j * d_state + n_id]; } float L_val = 0.0f; if (j <= i) { float pref_i = smem_prefix[i]; float pref_jm1 = (j > 0) ? smem_prefix[j-1] : 0.0f; L_val = expf(pref_i - pref_jm1); } smem_M[idx] = sum * L_val; } __syncthreads(); ``` **Step: Compute Y_diag = M @ X** ``` for (int idx = tid; idx < l*p; idx += blockDim.x) { int i = idx / d_head; // row in l int p_idx = idx % d_head; float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < l; j++) { sum += smem_M[i * l + j] * smem_X[j * d_head + p_idx]; } smem_Y_diag[idx] = sum; } __syncthreads(); ``` Note: we could optionally unroll the inner loop with `l=block_len=64`. Since block_len is a runtime param, we can't fully unroll unless it's a template parameter. But the compiler might unroll small constant loops if we pass it as a template. The problem says "Replace the forward computation of the PyTorch model below". Usually in these benchmarks, they instantiate the reference model, get its state_dict, load it into ModelNew, and compare outputs. Or they initialize ModelNew with the same parameters. Looking at `submit_kernel` and testing, the reference forward is run. ModelNew is supposed to be a drop-in replacement. The test harness likely does something like: ```python model_ref = Model(*get_init_inputs()) model_new = ModelNew(*get_init_inputs()) # Maybe copy parameters? inputs = get_inputs() out_ref = model_ref(*inputs) out_new = model_new(*inputs) # compare ``` But if it just initializes both randomly, they won't match. So there must be a mechanism to align parameters. Perhaps they copy state_dict, or they seed the RNG. The most robust approach is to ensure that `ModelNew` can accept a state dict from `Model`. For that, the parameter names must match and shapes must match. So we should keep parameters with the same names and shapes as the reference: `self.A`, `self.B`, `self.C` with shapes (b, seq_len, h), (b, seq_len, h, n), (b, seq_len, h, n). Then in forward, we can permute them on the fly: ```python A = self.A.permute(0, 2, 1).contiguous() # (b, h, seq_len) B = self.B.permute(0, 2, 1, 3).contiguous() # (b, h, seq_len, n) C = self.C.permute(0, 2, 1, 3).contiguous() X_perm = X.permute(0, 2, 1, 3).contiguous() # (b, h, seq_len, p) ``` Then call kernel. After kernel, permute Y back: ```python Y = Y_perm.permute(0, 2, 1, 3).contiguous() ``` These permutes are done every forward pass. The cost is small compared to the main computation (copying ~ 1GB total). However, with batch=2048, seq_len=128, n_heads=8: A: 2048*128*8 = 2M floats = 8MB. B+C: 2 * 2048*128*8*16 = 67M floats = 256MB. X: 2048*128*8*64 = 134M floats = 512MB. Y: same as X: 512MB. So total permute traffic: A(8*2) + B+C(256*2) + X(512*2) + Y(512*2) = 16 + 512 + 1024 + 1024 = ~2576MB. At 1.5TB/s, that's ~1.7ms. The kernel compute is maybe 2-3ms. So permutes add significant overhead. To avoid permutes, we can handle strided loads in the kernel. Let's think about reading directly from the original layout. Original layouts: A: (b, seq_len, h). Strides: stride0 = seq_len * h, stride1 = h, stride2 = 1. So A[b, t, h] = A_ptr[ b*seq_len*h + t*h + h_idx ]. B: (b, seq_len, h, n). Strides: stride0 = seq_len*h*n, stride1 = h*n, stride2 = n, stride3 = 1. B[b, t, h, n] = B_ptr[ b*seq_len*h*n + t*h*n + h_idx*n + n_idx ]. C: same as B. X: (b, seq_len, h, p). Strides: stride0 = seq_len*h*p, stride1 = h*p, stride2 = p, stride3 = 1. X[b, t, h, p] = X_ptr[ ... ]. Y: same as X. For a given (b, h), the positions across t are strided by h. For A: address = b*seq_len*h + h_idx + t*h. So contiguous in t with stride h. Not contiguous. For B: address = b*seq_len*h*n + h_idx*n + t*h*n. So contiguous in t with stride h*n. For X: address = b*seq_len*h*p + h_idx*p + t*h*p. Stride h*p. In the kernel, we can load with stride `n_heads`. For example, for a chunk starting at t0, thread tid loads: A[base + tid * n_heads] (if loading along t). But we need to load l elements. Actually, for thread tid, to load element i of A: `A[base_A + i * n_heads]`. Where base_A = b*seq_len*n_heads + h. This is a strided load with stride `n_heads` = 8. This is not coalesced across threads within a warp! Because consecutive threads load addresses spaced by 8 floats = 32 bytes. For a warp of 32 threads, the addresses are base, base+32, base+64, ... which is a stride of 32 bytes. This is actually not bad for L1 cache: each thread loads 4 bytes, but they are 32 bytes apart. A cache line is 128 bytes. So each load transaction might fetch 128 bytes, covering 4 threads. So a warp needs 8 transactions for 32 threads. That's 8x more than coalesced. For l=64, we have 2 warps loading A. Total transactions for A: 16. Very cheap. For B: stride is n_heads * d_state = 8*16 = 128 floats = 512 bytes. That's huge. Consecutive threads load 512 bytes apart. That's terrible for memory coalescing. Similarly X: stride is n_heads * d_head = 8*64 = 512 floats = 2048 bytes. Also terrible. So reading directly from original layout is bad for memory coalescing. It's much better to permute to contiguous layout. But we can do the permute inside the kernel using shared memory? No, we still need load from global memory. Alternatively, we can transpose the parameters once in `__init__`. But we need to handle parameter shapes for state dict loading. However, the test harness probably does not load a state dict. It likely just calls forward with random parameters. Let's check the problem statement: "Replace the forward computation of the PyTorch model below with a custom CUDA kernel. Your `ModelNew` class must: 1. Accept the same constructor arguments as `Model`. 2. Implement `forward()` with the same signature. 3. Produce numerically equivalent outputs at fp32 precision." It doesn't explicitly say they copy state dict. But to be safe, it's better to keep parameter shapes and names identical. However, if they just instantiate and run forward, the random init will be different. So they must copy parameters or use same seed. The most common approach in these benchmarks is: ```python model = Model(*args) model_new = ModelNew(*args) model_new.load_state_dict(model.state_dict()) ``` Or maybe: ```python with torch.no_grad(): for p1, p2 in zip(model.parameters(), model_new.parameters()): p2.copy_(p1) ``` If they do this, then `ModelNew` must have parameters with matching names and shapes. So we should keep `self.A`, `self.B`, `self.C` with the same shapes. But we can still create internal pre-permuted buffers in `forward` or `__init__`. In `__init__`, we can create pre-permuted views/contiguous copies and store them as attributes (not nn.Parameter, just buffers). Then in forward, we can use them. But if they load state dict after `__init__`, our internal buffers would be stale. Unless we recompute them every forward. In `forward`, we can compute: ```python A_perm = self.A.permute(0, 2, 1).contiguous() B_perm = self.B.permute(0, 2, 1, 3).contiguous() C_perm = self.C.permute(0, 2, 1, 3).contiguous() X_perm = X.permute(0, 2, 1, 3).contiguous() ``` This will cost ~1.7ms of memory traffic. It might be okay but let's see if we can avoid it. Actually, we can permute only once per forward pass for A,B,C,X. The kernel then runs. Then permute Y back. If the kernel runtime is ~2ms, adding 1.7ms overhead is a lot. But maybe the reference is even slower because it does many operations and allocations. Let's estimate reference runtime. The reference does: - rearrange X,A,B,C: 4 rearranges (views mostly, but A_blocks also permute). - cumsum on A. - segsum on A: creates L (67M elements). - einsum for Y_diag: 21.5B FLOPs. - einsum for decay_states and states. - cat, segsum on A_cumsum_last: tiny. - einsum for new_states: tiny. - einsum for Y_off: 4.3B FLOPs. - rearrange Y. PyTorch einsum for Y_diag on these sizes might take ~5-10ms because it likely uses a series of batched matmuls and tranposes, plus memory allocations for intermediates. The reference also allocates L (256MB), states (128MB), etc. So reference might be ~10-20ms. If our kernel takes ~2ms and permutes take ~2ms, total ~4ms, we are still faster. However, we can also avoid permuting X and Y by having the kernel handle transposed batch dimensions. Wait, for X: shape (b, seq_len, h, p). We can view it as (b*h, seq_len, p) with stride (seq_len*h*p, h*p, p, 1)? No. Let's think: if we view X as (b*h, seq_len, p), then the memory layout is actually (b, seq_len, h, p). The stride for the second dimension (seq_len) is h*p. So for a fixed (b,h), the sequence is strided by h*p. Not contiguous. If we instead view as (b, h, seq_len, p) via `permute`, we get contiguous sequences per (b,h). Is there a way to read non-contiguous sequences efficiently using vectorized loads? The stride for X along seq is h*p = 8*64 = 512 floats. That's 2048 bytes. For a warp, consecutive threads load t=0,1,2,...? No, if each thread loads one element at a different t, the addresses are spaced by 2048 bytes. That's very bad. However, if we have each thread load a full vector of p elements (i.e., one time step), then threads in a warp load t=0,1,2,... which are spaced by h*p = 2048 bytes. So still bad. But we can have each thread load a contiguous block of time steps? For example, thread 0 loads t=0..15, thread 1 loads t=16..31. Then addresses are interleaved and not coalesced either. The best approach is to transpose the tensor before the kernel. So we should do `.permute(0,2,1,3).contiguous()`. To minimize this overhead, we can use `torch.empty_strided` or `.as_strided`? No, permute is needed. Another idea: In `__init__`, after calling `super().__init__()`, we can override the parameters to be stored in the transposed format, and also register original shapes as buffers. But if they copy state dict, the state dict keys will be `A`, `B`, `C` but shapes are transposed. The loading would fail. Unless we keep parameters and create pre-transposed copies in forward on the fly. Since parameters are persistent, we can create them in `__init__` as non-parameter tensors: ```python self.register_buffer('A_t', torch.empty(batch_size, n_heads, seq_length)) self.register_buffer('B_t', torch.empty(batch_size, n_heads, seq_length, d_state)) self.register_buffer('C_t', torch.empty(batch_size, n_heads, seq_length, d_state)) ``` Then in `__init__`, after creating parameters, we copy? No, the random init will happen after `__init__`. If the test harness copies parameters from the reference model to ModelNew, it will load into `self.A`, `self.B`, `self.C`. We can then in `forward` compute `A_t = self.A.permute(0,2,1).contiguous()` every time. Since `self.A` is on GPU, `.permute` returns a view, `.contiguous()` triggers a copy. Alternatively, we can do the permute lazily and cache? If parameters don't change between forward calls (they don't in eval mode), we could do it once in `forward` the first time, but we don't know when state dict is loaded. We could override `load_state_dict` or `_load_from_state_dict` to also update transposed buffers. But that's complex. Let's just do on-the-fly permute in `forward`. It's simpler and robust. The overhead is acceptable given the compute. Actually, we can also permute parameters only (A,B,C) on the fly, and permute X and Y. Maybe we can fuse the X permute into the kernel by having the kernel read from the original layout but using `float4` loads? Let's see: X has shape (b,T,h,p). For a fixed (b,h), the p elements for time t are contiguous. So X[b,t,h,:] is contiguous p=64 floats = 256 bytes. Perfect for `float4` loads (16 bytes = 4 floats). For 64 floats, 16 `float4` loads. If we load time step t across the block, we need to read 64 floats per thread for p dimension? No, we can load X into shared memory with each thread loading a `float4` from X[b, tid, h, :]? But tid is thread index, not time index. Better: For loading X of shape (l,p) into shared memory, we can iterate over time steps. For a given time step t, the data is at offset base_X + t * (h*p) + h_idx * p. Consecutive threads loading the same time step but different p indices can load contiguous floats. So assign threads along the p dimension: p=64, block size=256. For time step t, we need to load 64 floats. We can assign 64 threads (one warp) to load p indices, and repeat for l=64 time steps. The 256 threads can be split into 4 warps, each handling one time step? Actually we can have threads load with stride blockDim.x. Let's formalize: We want to load X[t,p] for t=0..l-1, p=0..p-1. Address for (t,p) = base_X + t * (n_heads * d_head) + p. Since p dimension is contiguous with stride 1, we can have each thread load a `float4` (or float) along p, and loop over t. For example: ``` for (int t = 0; t < l; ++t) { for (int p_idx = tid; p_idx < d_head; p_idx += blockDim.x) { smem_X[t * d_head + p_idx] = X[base_X + t * (n_heads * d_head) + p_idx]; } } ``` Here, for a fixed t, threads with consecutive `tid` access consecutive `p_idx`, which are consecutive in memory. That's coalesced! Because X[b,t,h,:] is contiguous. Similarly for B and C: B[b,t,h,:] and C[b,t,h,:] are contiguous in n. For A: A[b,t,h] is a scalar, but consecutive t are strided by h. Loading A[t] with thread t is not coalesced. But A is only 64 scalars. We can load it into registers with one thread or have threads load with stride and then `__syncthreads()`. Even if uncoalesced, it's tiny. So here's the insight: We don't need to permute the whole tensor! We can load into shared memory in a coalesced way by having threads traverse the contiguous dimension (p or n) first, then the time dimension. Specifically, for X: ``` for (int t = 0; t < l; ++t) { int global_t = chunk_offset + t; int base_X_t = ((b * seq_len + global_t) * n_heads + h) * d_head; // original layout (b, t, h, p) for (int p_idx = tid; p_idx < d_head; p_idx += blockDim.x) { smem_X[t * d_head + p_idx] = X[base_X_t + p_idx]; } } ``` Wait, original layout: X[b, t, h, p]. So index = ((b * seq_len + t) * n_heads + h) * d_head + p. Thus base for chunk t: `((b * seq_len + global_t) * n_heads + h) * d_head`. For consecutive p_idx, memory is contiguous. So threads with consecutive tid access contiguous memory: coalesced! For B and C: ``` int base_B_t = ((b * seq_len + global_t) * n_heads + h) * d_state; for (int n_idx = tid; n_idx < d_state; n_idx += blockDim.x) { smem_B[t * d_state + n_idx] = B[base_B_t + n_idx]; } ``` Coalesced! For A: ``` int base_A_t = (b * seq_len + global_t) * n_heads + h; // scalar per thread t smem_A[t] = A[base_A_t]; // but need to load 64 scalars. We can have tid=0..63 load one each. for (int t = tid; t < l; t += blockDim.x) { int global_t = chunk_offset + t; smem_A[t] = A[(b * seq_len + global_t) * n_heads + h]; } ``` This is strided by n_heads in global memory. But only 64 loads per block. Negligible. Similarly, writing Y back: ``` for (int t = 0; t < l; ++t) { int global_t = chunk_offset + t; int base_Y_t = ((b * seq_len + global_t) * n_heads + h) * d_head; for (int p_idx = tid; p_idx < d_head; p_idx += blockDim.x) { Y[base_Y_t + p_idx] = smem_Y[t * d_head + p_idx]; } } ``` This is also coalesced. This is excellent! We can keep the original layout in Python and avoid all permutes. The kernel handles the indexing. Let's verify the index formula for the original layout. Reference shapes: - X: (batch_size, seq_length, n_heads, d_head) So X[b, t, h, p] is contiguous in p. stride_b = seq_length * n_heads * d_head stride_t = n_heads * d_head stride_h = d_head stride_p = 1 A: (batch_size, seq_length, n_heads) stride_b = seq_length * n_heads stride_t = n_heads stride_h = 1 B: (batch_size, seq_length, n_heads, d_state) strides same as X but with d_state instead of d_head. So base address for chunk at time t0: For X: `base = b * seq_len * n_heads * d_head + t0 * n_heads * d_head + h * d_head`. For time t: `addr = base + t * n_heads * d_head + p_idx`. In code: ``` int base_X = ((b * seq_len + chunk_offset) * n_heads + h) * d_head; ``` Then for time t: ``` int offset_t = t * n_heads * d_head; float val = X[base_X + offset_t + p_idx]; ``` Yes. A: ``` int base_A = ((b * seq_len + chunk_offset) * n_heads + h); for (int t = tid; t < l; t += blockDim.x) { smem_A[t] = A[base_A + t * n_heads]; } ``` This avoids permutes entirely! The only memory traffic is the inputs and outputs, plus no extra intermediate global memory. This is optimal. Let's rewrite the kernel with original layout indexing. **Kernel parameters:** ```cpp __global__ void ssd_forward_kernel( const float* __restrict__ A, const float* __restrict__ B, const float* __restrict__ C, const float* __restrict__ X, float* __restrict__ Y, int batch_size, int seq_len, int n_heads, int d_state, int d_head, int block_len, int num_chunks) ``` Inside: ``` int tid = threadIdx.x; int b = blockIdx.x / n_heads; int h = blockIdx.x % n_heads; int stride_t_A = n_heads; int stride_t_B = n_heads * d_state; int stride_t_C = n_heads * d_state; int stride_t_X = n_heads * d_head; int stride_t_Y = n_heads * d_head; int base_A_bh = (b * seq_len) * n_heads + h; int base_B_bh = ((b * seq_len) * n_heads + h) * d_state; int base_C_bh = base_B_bh; int base_X_bh = ((b * seq_len) * n_heads + h) * d_head; int base_Y_bh = base_X_bh; ``` Actually, seq_len is total length. Chunk offset is chunk * block_len. For chunk c: `int t0 = c * block_len;` `int base_A_chunk = base_A_bh + t0 * stride_t_A;` `int base_B_chunk = base_B_bh + t0 * stride_t_B;` etc. Let's verify: base_A_bh = (b * seq_len) * n_heads + h = b*seq_len*n_heads + h. For t0: + t0 * n_heads. So total = b*seq_len*n_heads + h + t0*n_heads = (b*seq_len + t0)*n_heads + h? Wait: The address of A[b, t, h] is (b * seq_len + t) * n_heads + h. So base_A_bh should be b * seq_len * n_heads + h. Then adding t * n_heads gives (b * seq_len + t) * n_heads + h. Correct. Similarly for B[b,t,h,n]: ((b * seq_len + t) * n_heads + h) * d_state + n. So base_B_bh = (b * seq_len * n_heads + h) * d_state. Then adding t * (n_heads * d_state) gives ((b*seq_len + t)*n_heads + h) * d_state. Correct. Good. **Loading loops:** For each chunk in 0..num_chunks-1: int t0 = chunk * block_len; int base_A = base_A_bh + t0 * stride_t_A; int base_B = base_B_bh + t0 * stride_t_B; int base_C = base_C_bh + t0 * stride_t_C; int base_X = base_X_bh + t0 * stride_t_X; int base_Y = base_Y_bh + t0 * stride_t_Y; // Load A for (int i = tid; i < block_len; i += blockDim.x) { smem_A[i] = A[base_A + i * stride_t_A]; } // Load B for (int i = tid; i < block_len * d_state; i += blockDim.x) { int t = i / d_state; int n = i % d_state; smem_B[i] = B[base_B + t * stride_t_B + n]; } // Load C for (int i = tid; i < block_len * d_state; i += blockDim.x) { int t = i / d_state; int n = i % d_state; smem_C[i] = C[base_C + t * stride_t_C + n]; } // Load X for (int i = tid; i < block_len * d_head; i += blockDim.x) { int t = i / d_head; int p = i % d_head; smem_X[i] = X[base_X + t * stride_t_X + p]; } __syncthreads(); **Compute prefix** if (tid == 0) { smem_prefix[0] = smem_A[0]; for (int i=1; i<block_len; i++) smem_prefix[i] = smem_prefix[i-1] + smem_A[i]; } __syncthreads(); **Compute M** // M is block_len x block_len for (int idx = tid; idx < block_len * block_len; idx += blockDim.x) { int i = idx / block_len; int j = idx % block_len; float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int n_id = 0; n_id < d_state; n_id++) { sum += smem_C[i * d_state + n_id] * smem_B[j * d_state + n_id]; } float L_val = 0.0f; if (j <= i) { float pref_i = smem_prefix[i]; float pref_jm1 = (j > 0) ? smem_prefix[j-1] : 0.0f; L_val = expf(pref_i - pref_jm1); } smem_M[idx] = sum * L_val; } __syncthreads(); **Compute Y_diag** for (int idx = tid; idx < block_len * d_head; idx += blockDim.x) { int i = idx / d_head; int p_idx = idx % d_head; float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < block_len; j++) { sum += smem_M[i * block_len + j] * smem_X[j * d_head + p_idx]; } smem_Y[idx] = sum; } __syncthreads(); **Compute intra_state** // First scale B by decay float A_last = smem_prefix[block_len-1]; for (int idx = tid; idx < block_len * d_state; idx += blockDim.x) { int j = idx / d_state; smem_B[idx] *= expf(A_last - smem_prefix[j]); } __syncthreads(); for (int idx = tid; idx < d_head * d_state; idx += blockDim.x) { int p_idx = idx / d_state; int n_id = idx % d_state; float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < block_len; j++) { sum += smem_X[j * d_head + p_idx] * smem_B[j * d_state + n_id]; } smem_intra[idx] = sum; } __syncthreads(); **Compute Y_off and accumulate** for (int idx = tid; idx < block_len * d_head; idx += blockDim.x) { int i = idx / d_head; int p_idx = idx % d_head; float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int n_id = 0; n_id < d_state; n_id++) { sum += smem_C[i * d_state + n_id] * smem_state[p_idx * d_state + n_id]; } smem_Y[idx] += sum * expf(smem_prefix[i]); } __syncthreads(); **Write Y** for (int idx = tid; idx < block_len * d_head; idx += blockDim.x) { int t = idx / d_head; int p_idx = idx % d_head; Y[base_Y + t * stride_t_Y + p_idx] = smem_Y[idx]; } __syncthreads(); **Update state** float decay_chunk = expf(A_last); for (int idx = tid; idx < d_head * d_state; idx += blockDim.x) { smem_state[idx] = decay_chunk * (smem_intra[idx] + smem_state[idx]); } __syncthreads(); End of chunk loop. We also need to zero-initialize smem_state before the loop. **Potential issues:** 1. Numerical precision. The reference uses `torch.exp` and `torch.cumsum`. In fp32, our cumsum is sequential (thread 0). This is exact for 64 elements. PyTorch cumsum is also sequential in each thread for small sizes? Actually PyTorch uses parallel scan which might have slightly different rounding. For 64 elements, the difference should be tiny. 2. `segsum` in the reference uses `-torch.inf` for upper triangle, `exp(-inf)=0`. We use `j <= i` conditional. Good. 3. `F.pad(A_cumsum[:,:,:,-1], (1,0))` adds a zero at the beginning. Our recurrence handles this by initializing state to 0 and using `decay_chunk = exp(A_last)`. This matches. 4. We need to make sure `smem_state` is correctly initialized to 0. We'll do that before the loop. 5. Shared memory bank conflicts. Our shared memory arrays are sized to avoid conflicts if possible. Since p=64, d_state=16, block_len=64, accesses might have some conflicts but shouldn't be terrible. Let's think about `smem_M` access pattern. When computing Y_diag, we read `smem_M[i*block_len + j]` where j varies across threads? For a fixed idx, `i` and `p_idx` vary. Threads with consecutive `idx` have same `i` if `idx` are within `d_head` range? Wait: idx = i * d_head + p_idx. With d_head=64, idx=0..63: i=0, p=0..63. So 64 consecutive threads have same i, different p. They all read the same row `i` of M, but different columns of X. Reading `smem_M[i*block_len + j]` for various j: all threads read the same row i, so they read `smem_M[i*block_len + j]` for j loop. The j loop is sequential in each thread. For a given j, all threads read the same element `smem_M[i*bl + j]`. That's a broadcast. No bank conflict. Reading `smem_X[j*d_head + p_idx]`: threads have different p_idx, so they read consecutive elements. That's coalesced in shared memory (if array is sized properly). Good. When computing M: each thread handles a pair (i,j). Consecutive threads may have different i and j. They read `smem_C[i*d_state + n]` and `smem_B[j*d_state + n]`. For a fixed n, threads read different elements. Since d_state=16, `i*d_state + n` might cause bank conflicts if many threads have same `i*d_state % 32`. With 256 threads and i varying, conflicts might be moderate. But since the dot product is only n=16, it's fine. When computing Y_off: similar to Y_diag but reading C and state. Threads with consecutive idx: same i, different p. They read `smem_C[i*d_state + n]` (same for all threads, broadcast). They read `smem_state[p_idx*d_state + n]` (different p, consecutive). Good. When computing intra_state: we read `smem_X[j*d_head + p_idx]` and `smem_B[j*d_state + n]`. For a fixed idx = p_idx * d_state + n_id. Consecutive threads? idx increments by 1: p_idx may stay same, n increments. They access smem_X with same j but different p? Actually j is loop variable. For a given j, threads access different p_idx and n_id. Since X is sized [block_len][d_head], access pattern might be okay. Overall, the kernel should be efficient. Let's think about block size. 256 threads is a good number (8 warps). Occupancy: 64KB shared memory per block. On A100, 164KB SMEM per SM. So 2 blocks per SM. 108 SMs * 2 = 216 blocks concurrently. Total blocks = 16384. So we need 16384 / 216 ≈ 76 waves. Each wave processes 2 chunks (since each block loops over chunks). For c=2, total work per block is 2 * (compute Y_diag + intra_state + Y_off + update). The compute per block is roughly 2 * (256 threads * (1024 + 256 + 1024 + 64) FLOPs) = 2 * 256 * 2368 ≈ 1.2M FLOPs per block. Total FLOPs = 16384 * 1.2M ≈ 19.7B FLOPs. This is slightly less than our earlier estimate because we didn't count M compute and prefix. Still in the ballpark. At ~10 TFLOPS, 19.7B FLOPs = 2ms. Memory traffic: inputs are loaded once, output written once. Input read: A(8MB)+B(128MB)+C(128MB)+X(512MB) = 776MB. Output write: Y(512MB). Total memory = 1.288GB. At 2TB/s, 0.64ms. So total maybe 2-3ms. Let's code this up carefully. We need to be careful with `load_inline`. We will write C++ code with the kernel and host wrapper. The host wrapper will be exposed via `functions=['ssd_forward']`. We need to include `<cuda_runtime.h>`, `<math.h>`, etc. Let's draft the C++ code. One concern: the kernel uses `block_len` as a runtime variable in loops. If we want unrolling for performance, we should template it. Let's write a templated kernel. Since the test parameters are block_len=64, d_state=16, d_head=64. We'll provide a fast path for those. Let's write: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math.h> template <int BLOCK_LEN, int D_STATE, int D_HEAD> __global__ void ssd_forward_kernel( const float* __restrict__ A, const float* __restrict__ B, const float* __restrict__ C, const float* __restrict__ X, float* __restrict__ Y, int batch_size, int seq_len, int n_heads) { int tid = threadIdx.x; int b = blockIdx.x / n_heads; int h = blockIdx.x % n_heads; int stride_t_A = n_heads; int stride_t_B = n_heads * D_STATE; int stride_t_C = n_heads * D_STATE; int stride_t_X = n_heads * D_HEAD; int stride_t_Y = n_heads * D_HEAD; int base_A_bh = b * seq_len * n_heads + h; int base_B_bh = (b * seq_len * n_heads + h) * D_STATE; int base_C_bh = base_B_bh; int base_X_bh = (b * seq_len * n_heads + h) * D_HEAD; int base_Y_bh = base_X_bh; int num_chunks = seq_len / BLOCK_LEN; // Shared memory extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* smem_A = smem; // [BLOCK_LEN] float* smem_prefix = smem_A + BLOCK_LEN; // [BLOCK_LEN] float* smem_B = smem_prefix + BLOCK_LEN; // [BLOCK_LEN * D_STATE] float* smem_C = smem_B + BLOCK_LEN * D_STATE; // [BLOCK_LEN * D_STATE] float* smem_X = smem_C + BLOCK_LEN * D_STATE; // [BLOCK_LEN * D_HEAD] float* smem_M = smem_X + BLOCK_LEN * D_HEAD; // [BLOCK_LEN * BLOCK_LEN] float* smem_Y = smem_M + BLOCK_LEN * BLOCK_LEN; // [BLOCK_LEN * D_HEAD] float* smem_intra = smem_Y + BLOCK_LEN * D_HEAD; // [D_HEAD * D_STATE] float* smem_state = smem_intra + D_HEAD * D_STATE; // [D_HEAD * D_STATE] // Zero initialize state for (int i = tid; i < D_HEAD * D_STATE; i += blockDim.x) { smem_state[i] = 0.0f; } __syncthreads(); for (int chunk = 0; chunk < num_chunks; ++chunk) { int t0 = chunk * BLOCK_LEN; int base_A = base_A_bh + t0 * stride_t_A; int base_B = base_B_bh + t0 * stride_t_B; int base_C = base_C_bh + t0 * stride_t_C; int base_X = base_X_bh + t0 * stride_t_X; int base_Y = base_Y_bh + t0 * stride_t_Y; // Load A for (int i = tid; i < BLOCK_LEN; i += blockDim.x) { smem_A[i] = A[base_A + i * stride_t_A]; } // Load B for (int i = tid; i < BLOCK_LEN * D_STATE; i += blockDim.x) { int t = i / D_STATE; int n = i % D_STATE; smem_B[i] = B[base_B + t * stride_t_B + n]; } // Load C for (int i = tid; i < BLOCK_LEN * D_STATE; i += blockDim.x) { int t = i / D_STATE; int n = i % D_STATE; smem_C[i] = C[base_C + t * stride_t_C + n]; } // Load X for (int i = tid; i < BLOCK_LEN * D_HEAD; i += blockDim.x) { int t = i / D_HEAD; int p = i % D_HEAD; smem_X[i] = X[base_X + t * stride_t_X + p]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute prefix sums of A if (tid == 0) { smem_prefix[0] = smem_A[0]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 1; i < BLOCK_LEN; ++i) { smem_prefix[i] = smem_prefix[i-1] + smem_A[i]; } } __syncthreads(); // Compute M = (C @ B^T) * L #pragma unroll for (int idx = tid; idx < BLOCK_LEN * BLOCK_LEN; idx += blockDim.x) { int i = idx / BLOCK_LEN; int j = idx % BLOCK_LEN; float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int n_id = 0; n_id < D_STATE; ++n_id) { sum += smem_C[i * D_STATE + n_id] * smem_B[j * D_STATE + n_id]; } float L_val = 0.0f; if (j <= i) { float pref_i = smem_prefix[i]; float pref_jm1 = (j > 0) ? smem_prefix[j-1] : 0.0f; L_val = expf(pref_i - pref_jm1); } smem_M[idx] = sum * L_val; } __syncthreads(); // Compute Y_diag = M @ X #pragma unroll for (int idx = tid; idx < BLOCK_LEN * D_HEAD; idx += blockDim.x) { int i = idx / D_HEAD; int p_idx = idx % D_HEAD; float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < BLOCK_LEN; ++j) { sum += smem_M[i * BLOCK_LEN + j] * smem_X[j * D_HEAD + p_idx]; } smem_Y[idx] = sum; } __syncthreads(); // Compute intra_state = X^T @ (B * decay) float A_last = smem_prefix[BLOCK_LEN - 1]; // Scale B by decay in-place for (int idx = tid; idx < BLOCK_LEN * D_STATE; idx += blockDim.x) { int j = idx / D_STATE; smem_B[idx] *= expf(A_last - smem_prefix[j]); } __syncthreads(); #pragma unroll for (int idx = tid; idx < D_HEAD * D_STATE; idx += blockDim.x) { int p_idx = idx / D_STATE; int n_id = idx % D_STATE; float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < BLOCK_LEN; ++j) { sum += smem_X[j * D_HEAD + p_idx] * smem_B[j * D_STATE + n_id]; } smem_intra[idx] = sum; } __syncthreads(); // Compute Y_off = exp(prefix) * (C @ state^T) and add to Y_diag #pragma unroll for (int idx = tid; idx < BLOCK_LEN * D_HEAD; idx += blockDim.x) { int i = idx / D_HEAD; int p_idx = idx % D_HEAD; float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int n_id = 0; n_id < D_STATE; ++n_id) { sum += smem_C[i * D_STATE + n_id] * smem_state[p_idx * D_STATE + n_id]; } smem_Y[idx] += sum * expf(smem_prefix[i]); } __syncthreads(); // Write Y for (int idx = tid; idx < BLOCK_LEN * D_HEAD; idx += blockDim.x) { int t = idx / D_HEAD; int p_idx = idx % D_HEAD; Y[base_Y + t * stride_t_Y + p_idx] = smem_Y[idx]; } __syncthreads(); // Update state: state = exp(A_last) * (intra + state) float decay_chunk = expf(A_last); for (int idx = tid; idx < D_HEAD * D_STATE; idx += blockDim.x) { smem_state[idx] = decay_chunk * (smem_intra[idx] + smem_state[idx]); } __syncthreads(); } } void ssd_forward( torch::Tensor A, torch::Tensor B, torch::Tensor C, torch::Tensor X, torch::Tensor Y, int batch_size, int seq_len, int n_heads, int d_state, int d_head, int block_len) { int num_blocks = batch_size * n_heads; int threads = 256; int smem_size = 0; // Calculate shared memory size smem_size = (2 * block_len + 2 * block_len * d_state + 2 * block_len * d_head + block_len * block_len + 2 * d_head * d_state) * sizeof(float); cudaStream_t stream = at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream(); if (block_len == 64 && d_state == 16 && d_head == 64) { ssd_forward_kernel<64, 16, 64><<<num_blocks, threads, smem_size, stream>>>( A.data_ptr<float>(), B.data_ptr<float>(), C.data_ptr<float>(), X.data_ptr<float>(), Y.data_ptr<float>(), batch_size, seq_len, n_heads); } else { // Fallback: use the generic version? For now, we don't have a generic version. // We can instantiate a few common ones or just call a generic kernel. // Let's write a generic kernel too to be safe. // Actually, we can reuse the same code but with runtime loops. // To keep it simple and fast for the benchmark, let's just handle the exact params. // But we need a fallback for safety. } } ``` Wait, we need a fallback generic kernel. Let's write the generic version. ```cpp __global__ void ssd_forward_kernel_generic( const float* __restrict__ A, const float* __restrict__ B, const float* __restrict__ C, const float* __restrict__ X, const float* __restrict__ initial_state, float* __restrict__ Y, int batch_size, int seq_len, int n_heads, int d_state, int d_head, int block_len, int num_chunks) { int tid = threadIdx.x; int b = blockIdx.x / n_heads; int h = blockIdx.x % n_heads; int stride_t_A = n_heads; int stride_t_B = n_heads * d_state; int stride_t_C = n_heads * d_state; int stride_t_X = n_heads * d_head; int stride_t_Y = n_heads * d_head; int base_A_bh = b * seq_len * n_heads + h; int base_B_bh = (b * seq_len * n_heads + h) * d_state; int base_C_bh = base_B_bh; int base_X_bh = (b * seq_len * n_heads + h) * d_head; int base_Y_bh = base_X_bh; extern __shared__ float smem[]; int offset = 0; float* smem_A = smem + offset; offset += block_len; float* smem_prefix = smem + offset; offset += block_len; float* smem_B = smem + offset; offset += block_len * d_state; float* smem_C = smem + offset; offset += block_len * d_state; float* smem_X = smem + offset; offset += block_len * d_head; float* smem_M = smem + offset; offset += block_len * block_len; float* smem_Y = smem + offset; offset += block_len * d_head; float* smem_intra = smem + offset; offset += d_head * d_state; float* smem_state = smem + offset; offset += d_head * d_state; // Initialize state if (initial_state != nullptr) { int base_init = (b * n_heads + h) * d_head * d_state; for (int i = tid; i < d_head * d_state; i += blockDim.x) { smem_state[i] = initial_state[base_init + i]; } } else { for (int i = tid; i < d_head * d_state; i += blockDim.x) { smem_state[i] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); for (int chunk = 0; chunk < num_chunks; ++chunk) { int t0 = chunk * block_len; int base_A = base_A_bh + t0 * stride_t_A; int base_B = base_B_bh + t0 * stride_t_B; int base_C = base_C_bh + t0 * stride_t_C; int base_X = base_X_bh + t0 * stride_t_X; int base_Y = base_Y_bh + t0 * stride_t_Y; // Load A for (int i = tid; i < block_len; i += blockDim.x) { smem_A[i] = A[base_A + i * stride_t_A]; } // Load B for (int i = tid; i < block_len * d_state; i += blockDim.x) { int t = i / d_state; int n = i % d_state; smem_B[i] = B[base_B + t * stride_t_B + n]; } // Load C for (int i = tid; i < block_len * d_state; i += blockDim.x) { int t = i / d_state; int n = i % d_state; smem_C[i] = C[base_C + t * stride_t_C + n]; } // Load X for (int i = tid; i < block_len * d_head; i += blockDim.x) { int t = i / d_head; int p = i % d_head; smem_X[i] = X[base_X + t * stride_t_X + p]; } __syncthreads(); // prefix sum if (tid == 0) { smem_prefix[0] = smem_A[0]; for (int i = 1; i < block_len; ++i) { smem_prefix[i] = smem_prefix[i-1] + smem_A[i]; } } __syncthreads(); // Compute M for (int idx = tid; idx < block_len * block_len; idx += blockDim.x) { int i = idx / block_len; int j = idx % block_len; float sum = 0.0f; for (int n_id = 0; n_id < d_state; ++n_id) { sum += smem_C[i * d_state + n_id] * smem_B[j * d_state + n_id]; } float L_val = 0.0f; if (j <= i) { float pref_i = smem_prefix[i]; float pref_jm1 = (j > 0) ? smem_prefix[j-1] : 0.0f; L_val = expf(pref_i - pref_jm1); } smem_M[idx] = sum * L_val; } __syncthreads(); // Compute Y_diag for (int idx = tid; idx < block_len * d_head; idx += blockDim.x) { int i = idx / d_head; int p_idx = idx % d_head; float sum = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < block_len; ++j) { sum += smem_M[i * block_len + j] * smem_X[j * d_head + p_idx]; } smem_Y[idx] = sum; } __syncthreads(); // Compute intra_state float A_last = smem_prefix[block_len - 1]; for (int idx = tid; idx < block_len * d_state; idx += blockDim.x) { int j = idx / d_state; smem_B[idx] *= expf(A_last - smem_prefix[j]); } __syncthreads(); for (int idx = tid; idx < d_head * d_state; idx += blockDim.x) { int p_idx = idx / d_state; int n_id = idx % d_state; float sum = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < block_len; ++j) { sum += smem_X[j * d_head + p_idx] * smem_B[j * d_state + n_id]; } smem_intra[idx] = sum; } __syncthreads(); // Compute Y_off for (int idx = tid; idx < block_len * d_head; idx += blockDim.x) { int i = idx / d_head; int p_idx = idx % d_head; float sum = 0.0f; for (int n_id = 0; n_id < d_state; ++n_id) { sum += smem_C[i * d_state + n_id] * smem_state[p_idx * d_state + n_id]; } smem_Y[idx] += sum * expf(smem_prefix[i]); } __syncthreads(); // Write Y for (int idx = tid; idx < block_len * d_head; idx += blockDim.x) { int t = idx / d_head; int p_idx = idx % d_head; Y[base_Y + t * stride_t_Y + p_idx] = smem_Y[idx]; } __syncthreads(); // Update state float decay_chunk = expf(A_last); for (int idx = tid; idx < d_head * d_state; idx += blockDim.x) { smem_state[idx] = decay_chunk * (smem_intra[idx] + smem_state[idx]); } __syncthreads(); } } ``` Then the wrapper: ```cpp void ssd_forward( torch::Tensor A, torch::Tensor B, torch::Tensor C, torch::Tensor X, torch::Tensor initial_state, torch::Tensor Y, int batch_size, int seq_len, int n_heads, int d_state, int d_head, int block_len) { int num_blocks = batch_size * n_heads; const float* init_ptr = nullptr; if (initial_state.defined() && initial_state.numel() > 0) { init_ptr = initial_state.data_ptr<float>(); } cudaStream_t stream = at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream(); ssd_forward_kernel_generic<<<num_blocks, threads, smem_size, stream>>>( A.data_ptr<float>(), B.data_ptr<float>(), C.data_ptr<float>(), X.data_ptr<float>(), init_ptr, Y.data_ptr<float>(), batch_size, seq_len, n_heads, d_state, d_head, block_len, num_chunks); } ``` Wait, we need to handle `initial_state` being an optional tensor. smem + off; off += block_len;\n float* smem_B = smem + off; off += block_len * d_state;\n float* smem_C = smem + off; off += block_len * d_state;\n float* smem_X = smem + off; off += block_len * d_head;\n float* smem_M = smem + off; off += block_len * block_len;\n float* smem_Y = smem + off; off += block_len * d_head;\n float* smem_intra = smem + off; off += d_head * d_state;\n float* smem_state = smem + off; off += d_head * d_state;\n\n // initialise state (carry-over across chunks)\n if (initial_state != nullptr) {\n int base_init = (b * n_heads + h) * d_head * d_state;\n for (int i = tid; i < d_head * d_state; i += blockDim.x) {\n smem_state[i] = initial_state[base_init + i];\n }\n } else {\n for (int i = tid; i < d_head * d_state; i += blockDim.x) {\n smem_state[i] = stride_t_B;\n int base_C = base_C_bh + t0 * stride_t_C;\n int base_X = base_X_bh + t0 * stride_t_X;\n int base_Y = base_Y_bh + t0 * stride_t_Y;\n\n // load chunk data\n for (int i = tid; i <
