for (int i = tid; i < block_len * d_head; i += blockDim.x) {\n int t = i / d_head;\n int p = i % d_head;\n smem_X[i] = X[base_X + t * stride_t_X + p];\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n // prefix sum of A (inclusive)\n if (tid == 0) {\n smem_prefix[0] = smem_A[0];\n for (int i = 1; i < block_len; ++i) {\n smem_prefix[i] = smem_prefix[i-1] + smem_A[i];\n }\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n // M = (C @ B^T) * L (int n_id = 0; n_id < d_state; ++n_id) {\n sum += smem_C[i * d_state + n_id] * smem_B[j * d_state + n_id];\n }\n float L_val = 0.0f;\n if (j <= i) {\n L_val = expf(smem_prefix[i] - smem_prefix[j]);\n }\n smem_M[idx] = sum * L_val;\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n // Y_diag = M @ X\n for (int idx = tid; idx < block_len * d_head; idx += blockDim.x) {\n int i = idx / d_head;\n int p_idx = idx % d_head;\n float sum = 0.0f;\n for (int j = 0; j < block_len; ++j) {\n sum += smem_M[i * block_len + j] * smem_X[j * d_head + p_idx];\n }\n smem_Y[idx] = sum;\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n // intra_state = X^T @ (B * decay) , decay[j] = exp(A_last - prefix[j])\n float A_last = smem_prefix[block_len - 1];\n for (int idx = tid; idx < block_len * d_state; idx += blockDim.x) {\n int j = idx / d_state;\n smem_B[idx] *= expf(A_last - smem_prefix[j]);\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n for (int idx = tid; idx < d_head * d_state; idx += blockDim.x) {\n int p_idx = idx / d_state;\n int n_id = idx % d_state;\n float sum = 0.0f;\n for (int j = 0; j < block_len; ++j) {\n sum += smem_X[j * d_head + p_idx] * smem_B[j * d_state + n_id];\n }\n smem_intra[idx] = sum;\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n // Y_off = exp(prefix) * (C @ state^T) and accumulate into Y_diag\n for (int idx = tid; idx < block_len * d_head; idx += blockDim.x) {\n int i = idx / d_head;\n int p_idx = idx % d_head;\n float sum = 0.0f;\n for (int n_id = 0; n_id < d_state; ++n_id) {\n sum += smem_C[i * d_state + n_id] * smem_state[p_idx * d_state + n_id];\n }\n smem_Y[idx] += sum * expf(smem_prefix[i]);\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n // write output\n for reasoning
We need to fix two issues: 1. The `ssd_forward` function isn't declared. It's because with `load_inline`, the functions argument looks at `cpp_sources` for the declaration, but if passed empty, there's no declaration. Wait, actually the issue is that `ssd_forward` is declared in the CUDA source but it's not being seen by the generated pybind11 module. Actually, the error is: ``` scratch/.../ssd_cuda/main.cpp: In function 'void pybind11_init_ssd_cuda(pybind11::module_&)': ...main.cpp:4:50: error: 'ssd_forward' was not declared in this scope ``` The generated `main.cpp` tries to wrap `ssd_forward` but there is no declaration in `cpp_sources`. So we must provide the C++ declaration of `ssd_forward` in the `cpp_sources` argument. The pybind11 code auto-generates from `functions=[...]`, and it needs to know the function signature in `cpp_sources`. It's not enough to have it only in the CUDA source. We'll add a C++ header-like declaration in `cpp_sources`. 2. The `at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream()` is not found in some PyTorch contexts in `load_inline`. We can just pass `0` for the default stream, or avoid specifying the stream by passing `0`. The simplest way is to drop the `cudaStream_t` parameter and use the default stream by passing `0`. We can call the kernel with just `<<<num_blocks, threads, smem_size>>>` if we omit the stream argument? Actually kernel syntax requires either 3 or 4 arguments; you can do `<<<blocks, threads, smem>>>` which uses default stream. Or we can try `c10::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream()` or `at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream().stream()` or `thrust::cuda::par`... but easier to just use `<<<blocks, threads, smem_size>>>()`. Let's fix both issues by putting `cpp_sources` with a declaration: ```c++ #include <torch/extension.h> void ssd_forward( torch::Tensor A, torch::Tensor B, torch::Tensor C, torch::Tensor X, torch::Tensor initial_state, torch::Tensor Y, int batch_size, int seq_len, int n_heads, int d_state, int d_head, int block_len); ``` And in the CUDA source, we include <torch/extension.h>. Remove the `cudaStream_t` usage and just launch with default stream. Actually `torch/extension.h` in the CUDA source includes `torch/all.h` which includes `ATen/ATen.h`, so using default stream is fine. Let's also consider correctness: The reference model doesn't actually pass initial_states in the `get_inputs()` call (inputs is just X, forward takes X and optional initial_states). But we should support it. Our `initial_state` parameter is optional. Let me re-examine the reference computation to ensure numerical equivalence. Reference: ```python class Model(nn.Module): def __init__(self, batch_size, seq_length, n_heads, d_head, d_state, block_len=64): ... def segsum(self, x): T = x.size(-1) x_cumsum = torch.cumsum(x, dim=-1) x_segsum = x_cumsum[..., :, None] - x_cumsum[..., None, :] mask = torch.tril(torch.ones(T, T, device=x.device, dtype=bool), diagonal=0) x_segsum = x_segsum.masked_fill(~mask, -torch.inf) return x_segsum def forward(self, X, initial_states=None): X_blocks, A_blocks, B_blocks, C_blocks = [ rearrange(x, "b (c l) ... -> b c l ...", l=self.block_len) for x in (X, self.A, self.B, self.C) ] A_blocks = rearrange(A_blocks, "b c l h -> b h c l") A_cumsum = torch.cumsum(A_blocks, dim=-1) # 1. diag L = torch.exp(self.segsum(A_blocks)) Y_diag = torch.einsum("bclhn,bcshn,bhcls,bcshp->bclhp", C_blocks, B_blocks, L, X_blocks) # 2. intra-chunk states decay_states = torch.exp((A_cumsum[:, :, :, -1:] - A_cumsum)) states = torch.einsum("bclhn,bhcl,bclhp->bchpn", B_blocks, decay_states, X_blocks) # 3. inter-chunk recurrence if initial_states is None: initial_states = torch.zeros_like(states[:, :1]) states = torch.cat([initial_states, states], dim=1) decay_chunk = torch.exp(self.segsum(F.pad(A_cumsum[:, :, :, -1], (1, 0)))) new_states = torch.einsum("bhzc,bchpn->bzhpn", decay_chunk, states) states = new_states[:, :-1] # 4. state-to-output state_decay_out = torch.exp(A_cumsum) Y_off = torch.einsum('bclhn,bchpn,bhcl->bclhp', C_blocks, states, state_decay_out) Y = rearrange(Y_diag + Y_off, "b c l h p -> b (c l) h p") return Y ``` Let's parse the shapes carefully and verify the formulas. Input dimensions: batch_size, seq_length, n_heads, d_head. A: (batch, seq_length, n_heads) B: (batch, seq_length, n_heads, d_state) C: (batch, seq_length, n_heads, d_state) X: (batch, seq_length, n_heads, d_head) After rearrange to blocks with `l=block_len`: `X_blocks` etc have shape `(batch, num_chunks, block_len, n_heads, d_head)` (for X) or d_state for B/C. `A` is `(batch, num_chunks, block_len, n_heads)`. Then `rearrange(A_blocks, "b c l h -> b h c l")` gives `(batch, n_heads, num_chunks, block_len)`. `A_cumsum` over last dim: `(batch, n_heads, num_chunks, block_len)`. `segsum(A_blocks)`: for each head and chunk, create a lower-triangular matrix `M` of size (block_len, block_len) where M[i,j] = sum_{k=j}^{i} A[..., k] for i>=j, else -inf. `torch.exp(self.segsum(A_blocks))`: lower triangular matrix where entry (i,j) is exp(sum_{k=j+1 to i} A[...,k])? Let's check: If `x_cumsum[i] = sum_{k=0..i} x[k]`. Then `x_segsum[i,j] = x_cumsum[i] - x_cumsum[j] = sum_{k=j+1..i} x[k]`. If i>=j. For j=i, it's 0. So L[i,j] = exp(sum_{k=j+1..i} A[k]) for i>j, and exp(0) = 1 for i=j. In the reference: `Y_diag = torch.einsum("bclhn,bcshn,bhcls,bcshp->bclhp", C_blocks, B_blocks, L, X_blocks)` This means: For each batch b, chunk c, head h, time step l (which is `i`), and output dim p, Y_diag[b,c,l,h,p] = sum_{s=0..l} sum_{n=0..d_state-1} C[b,c,l,h,n] * B[b,c,s,h,n] * L[b,h,c,l,s] * X[b,c,s,h,p] Because `einsum` contracts on `s` and `n`. The `l` index of L corresponds to the row i and `s` to column j. This is correct: for `s > l`, L is `-inf` -> exp is 0, so effectively sum up to l. Good. `decay_states = torch.exp((A_cumsum[:, :, :, -1:] - A_cumsum))` Shape: `(batch, n_heads, num_chunks, block_len)`. For each position t in chunk, it's exp(sum_{k=t..end} A[k]?) `A_cumsum[-1]` is the sum over the whole chunk. `A_cumsum[-1:] - A_cumsum[t]` = sum_{k=t+1..end} A[k]. So `decay_states` = exp(sum_{k=t+1..end} A[k]). Let's call this `decay(t)`. `states = torch.einsum("bclhn,bhcl,bclhp->bchpn", B_blocks, decay_states, X_blocks)` This computes intra-chunk state for each chunk. Sum over l and n? Wait, `bclhn,bhcl,bclhp->bchpn`. Indices: b, c, h, p, n. The contracted index is l. states[b,c,h,p,n] = sum_{l=0..block_len-1} B[b,c,l,h,n] * decay[b,h,c,l] * X[b,c,l,h,p] This is exactly the "intra-chunk" state: it's B^T (weighted by decay) multiply X. But note: `einsum` uses `bclhn` for B and `bclhp` for X. So sum over l and n? Actually output has n, so n is from B. Sum over l. Yes. `states[b,c,h,p,n] = \sum_{l} B_{b,c,l,h,n} * decay_{b,h,c,l} * X_{b,c,l,h,p}` `if initial_states is None: initial_states = torch.zeros_like(states[:, :1])` `states = torch.cat([initial_states, states], dim=1)` -> `(batch, num_chunks+1, n_heads, d_head, d_state)`? Wait, `states` shape is `(batch, num_chunks, n_heads, d_head, d_state)`? Actually from einsum output order: `b c h p n`. That is `(batch, num_chunks, n_heads, d_head, d_state)`. Then `states[:, :1]` shape is `(batch, 1, n_heads, d_head, d_state)`. `torch.cat(..., dim=1)` gives `(batch, num_chunks+1, n_heads, d_head, d_state)`. `decay_chunk = torch.exp(self.segsum(F.pad(A_cumsum[:, :, :, -1], (1, 0))))` `A_cumsum[:, :, :, -1]` is the final prefix sum of each chunk, shape `(batch, n_heads, num_chunks)`. `F.pad(..., (1,0))` adds a zero at the beginning? Yes, padding last dim by 1 on left. So shape is `(batch, n_heads, num_chunks+1)`. The pad adds zero, which corresponds to initial cumulative sum before first chunk. `segsum` on this gives a lower triangular matrix of size `(num_chunks+1, num_chunks+1)` where entry (z,c) = exp(sum_{k=c+1..z} A_cumsum_last[k]) for z>=c. Note: sum of A_cumsum_last from c+1 to z is just sum of total chunk log increments from chunk c+1 to z. This is the inter-chunk decay. `new_states = torch.einsum("bhzc,bchpn->bzhpn", decay_chunk, states)` This is a matrix multiplication along chunk dimension. `new_states[b,z,h,p,n] = sum_{c=0..z} decay_chunk[b,h,z,c] * states[b,c,h,p,n]` This is like a prefix scan with decay weights. Because the mask ensures only c<=z contributes. After doing this, `states = new_states[:, :-1]` -> shape `(batch, num_chunks, n_heads, d_head, d_state)`. `state_decay_out = torch.exp(A_cumsum)` -> `(batch, n_heads, num_chunks, block_len)` where entry t is exp(prefix_sum up to t). `Y_off = torch.einsum('bclhn,bchpn,bhcl->bclhp', C_blocks, states, state_decay_out)` `Y_off[b,c,l,h,p] = sum_{n=0..d_state-1} C[b,c,l,h,n] * states[b,c,h,p,n] * exp(A_cumsum[b,h,c,l])` This is the state-to-output for each chunk using the *updated* state after inter-chunk recurrence. So, the combined operation per chunk `(b,c,h)` is: 1. Diagonal term: `L[i,j] = exp(prefix[i] - prefix[j])` for `j<=i`. 2. Off-diagonal term: `Y_off[i] = exp(prefix[i]) * C[i] @ state`, where `state` is the accumulated state from previous chunks and current chunk contributions. Let's verify our simplified kernel logic against this. My kernel processes heads. For each head `h` and batch `b`, it processes chunks `c=0..num_chunks-1` sequentially. Inside each chunk: - Load A[t], B[t,n], C[t,n], X[t,p] for t=0..block_len-1. - Compute inclusive prefix of A: `prefix[t]`. (t=0..L-1): prefix[t] = sum_{k=0..t} A[k]. This matches `A_cumsum`. - Compute M (L matrix): `M[i,j] = (C[i] dot B[j]) * exp(prefix[i] - prefix[j])` for j<=i. This matches the diagonal term because `L[i,j] = exp(prefix[i] - prefix[j])`, but note: the reference L[i,j] from `segsum(A_blocks)` is `exp(x_cumsum[i] - x_cumsum[j])` where x_cumsum is inclusive. Since our prefix is inclusive, `prefix[i] - prefix[j] = sum_{k=j+1..i} A[k]`. But wait: in `segsum`, `x_cumsum` is cumulative sum of the input A (which are the original A values, not prefix). And `x_cumsum[i] - x_cumsum[j]` for i>=j is `sum_{k=j+1..i} A[k]`. If j=i, it's 0. Our `prefix[i] - prefix[j]` is `sum_{k=0..i} A[k] - sum_{k=0..j} A[k] = sum_{k=j+1..i} A[k]`. This is the same! Good. So `M[i,j] = (C_i . B_j) * exp(prefix[i] - prefix[j])` for j<=i. This matches L in reference times C and B. - Y_diag = M @ X: Y_diag[i,p] = sum_{j=0..i} M[i,j] * X[j,p]. Correct. - Intra-chunk state computation: Reference: `states_intra = \sum_l B[l] * decay(l) * X[l]^T`? Wait, `states = einsum("bclhn,bhcl,bclhp->bchpn", B, decay_states, X)`. Correct. - Update and use state for Y_off: Reference: `Y_off[i] = exp(prefix[i]) * C[i] @ state`, where state is the result of inter-chunk recurrence. However, we can precompute `C[i]` and reuse it. Or we can store B and X in shared memory, and C in shared memory, so the dot product is fast. If we precompute `exp_prefix[t] = exp(prefix[t])` for all t, then `exp(prefix[i]-prefix[j]) = exp_prefix[i] * (1/exp_prefix[j])`. We can precompute `inv_exp_prefix[t]` too. 64 divisions per thread block. Then each j loop iteration does just 2 loads and 1 multiply. This is optimal. So add `smem_exp_prefix[64]` and `smem_inv_exp_prefix[64]`. That's 128 floats = 512 bytes. Negligible. Let's revise the shared memory layout: - `prefix[64]` - `exp_prefix[64]` - `inv_exp_prefix[64]` (can reuse a temp buffer or just compute div on the fly, but let's keep it simple) Actually we can just precompute `exp_prefix[j]` and compute `exp_prefix[i] / exp_prefix[j]`. Division is about 4x slower than multiplication but we do it 64 times per thread? No, per thread block: 64 divisions (one for each j) if we compute `inv_exp_prefix` in parallel (tid 0..63 compute one each). Then each thread in the j loop does one division? No, each thread does `exp_prefix_i / exp_prefix_j`. But all threads in a warp have the same i, so `exp_prefix_i` is broadcast. They read `exp_prefix_j` (broadcast if all threads same j? But j varies in loop, all threads same j). So the division is scalar and broadcast to all threads. However, the compiler will likely keep `exp_prefix_i` in a register and do `val = exp_prefix_i / exp_prefix[j]`. This is one scalar division per j. Division per j might still be expensive. Better to precompute `inv_exp_prefix[j]`. With `inv_exp_prefix`, each j loop iteration does: `mul = exp_prefix_i * inv_exp_prefix[j]`. 1 mul. The precomputation of `inv_exp_prefix` uses `__frcp_rn` or just `1.0f / exp_prefix[j]`. 64 such divisions per block. Very cheap. But we do need another 64 floats in smem for `inv_exp_prefix`. Or we can compute `inv_exp_prefix[j]` on the fly inside the j loop: `1.0f / exp_prefix[j]`. That's 64 scalar divisions per thread! Since all threads in warp do the same j, it's really one scalar division per j per warp (lockstep). But the compiler can't know that, though the hw might execute it once? No, each lane computes `1.0f / exp_prefix[j]`. On A100, scalar division in each lane is still a division instruction. Throughput is low. So precomputing `inv_exp_prefix` in shared memory by a subset of threads is better. Shared memory reallocation: - prefix: 64 - B: 64*16 = 1024 - C: 64*16 = 1024 - X: 64*64 = 4096 - state: 64*16 = 1024 (transposed) - intra: 64*16 = 1024 (transposed) - inv_exp_prefix: 64 Total: 64 + 1024 + 1024 + 4096 + 1024 + 1024 + 64 = 8420 floats = 33680 bytes. Let's write the full Python code. ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cpp_sources = ''' #include <torch/extension.h> void ssd_forward( torch::Tensor A, torch::Tensor B, torch::Tensor C, torch::Tensor X, torch::Tensor initial_state, torch::Tensor Y, int batch_size, int seq_len, int n_heads, int d_state, int d_head, int block_len); The output mismatch is huge, so there are bugs in the kernel logic. Let me carefully re-derive the algorithm and understand the expected shapes and operations. Looking at the reference code: ```python # 1. Compute diagonal block outputs L = torch.exp(self.segsum(A_blocks)) Y_diag = torch.einsum("bclhn,bcshn,bhcls,bcshp->bclhp", C_blocks, B_blocks, L, X_blocks) ``` `A_blocks` shape: `(b, c, l, h)` after rearrange `b c l h -> b h c l` actually wait: ```python A_blocks = rearrange(A_blocks, "b c l h -> b h c l") ``` So after this, `A_blocks` is `(b, h, c, l)`. Then `segsum`: ```python def segsum(self, x): # x: shape with last dim T x_cumsum = torch.cumsum(x, dim=-1) x_segsum = x_cumsum[..., :, None] - x_cumsum[..., None, :] mask = torch.tril(torch.ones(T, T, device=x.device, dtype=bool), diagonal=0) x_segsum = x_segsum.masked_fill(~mask, -torch.inf) return x_segsum ``` For `A_blocks: (b, h, c, l)`, `segsum` operates on last dim `l`, producing `(b, h, c, l, l)` where `L[i,j] = exp(cumsum(A[:i]) - cumsum(A[:j]))` for j <= i, else -inf. `L[b,h,c,s,l]` is exp of prefix[i] - prefix[j] for j<=i. Then `Y_diag = einsum("bclhn,bcshn,bhcls,bcshp->bclhp", C, B, L, X)` Wait, indices: - C: b, c, l, h, n (shape b,c,l,h,d_state) - B: b, c, s, h, n (s instead of l) - L: b, h, c, s, l - X: b, c, s, h, p So `Y_diag[b,c,l,h,p] = sum_{s,n} C[b,c,l,h,n] * B[b,c,s,h,n] * L[b,h,c,s,l] * X[b,c,s,h,p]` Wait, that's not what I expected. `L` index is `s, l`, not `l, s`. Let me double-check: `L = torch.exp(self.segsum(A_blocks))`. A_blocks is `(b, h, c, l)`. segsum produces `(b, h, c, l, l)` where last two dims are `(T, T)`. `x_cumsum[..., :, None] - x_cumsum[..., None, :]`. `[..., :, None]` means last dim becomes first of the two new dims. So `x_segsum[..., i, j] = x_cumsum[..., i] - x_cumsum[..., j]`. Then `L[i, j]` = exp(prefix[i] - prefix[j]) for j <= i. Then `bhcls` corresponds to `b, h, c, l(chunk_idx?), s(seq_idx?)`. Wait, `cls` - c, l, s. But L has shape (..., l, l). So `l` and `s` both range over block_len. Usually `l` is the output position, `s` is the input position. So `L[..., l, s] = exp(prefix[l] - prefix[s])` for s <= l. So for output `Y_diag[b,c,l,h,p]`: `sum_{s=0}^{l} sum_n C[b,c,l,h,n] * B[b,c,s,h,n] * exp(prefix[l]-prefix[s]) * X[b,c,s,h,p]` Wait, but the einsum string uses `cls` for L. Let me check: "bclhn,bcshn,bhcls,bcshp->bclhp" - C: b, c, l, h, n - B: b, c, s, h, n - L: b, h, c, l, s - X: b, c, s, h, p So `L[b,h,c,l,s]` corresponds to `exp(prefix[l] - prefix[s])`. For `s > l`, L is -inf, exp is 0. So effectively s from 0 to l. So `Y_diag[l,p] = sum_{s=0}^{l} [sum_n C[l,n] * B[s,n]] * exp(prefix[l]-prefix[s]) * X[s,p]` Yes, this matches. `L[b,h,c,l,s] = exp(prefix[l] - prefix[s])`. Then `decay_states = torch.exp((A_cumsum[:, :, :, -1:] - A_cumsum))` A_cumsum is `(b, h, c, l)` cumsum along last dim. `A_cumsum[:, :, :, -1:]` is `(b, h, c, 1)` - the last element of cumsum for each chunk. `A_cumsum[:, :, :, -1:] - A_cumsum` is `(b, h, c, l)`. For position j in chunk: `last_prefix - prefix[j]`. `decay_states[b,h,c,j] = exp(last_prefix - prefix[j])`. `states = torch.einsum("bclhn,bhcl,bclhp->bchpn", B_blocks, decay_states, X_blocks)` - B: b, c, l, h, n - decay: b, h, c, l - X: b, c, l, h, p Output: b, c, h, p, n `states[b,c,h,p,n] = sum_l B[b,c,l,h,n] * exp(last_prefix - prefix[l]) * X[b,c,l,h,p]` Then inter-chunk recurrence: ```python if initial_states is None: initial_states = torch.zeros_like(states[:, :1]) states = torch.cat([initial_states, states], dim=1) ``` states is now `(b, c+1, h, p, n)` where dim 1 is chunk index. `decay_chunk = torch.exp(self.segsum(F.pad(A_cumsum[:, :, :, -1], (1, 0))))` `A_cumsum[:, :, :, -1]` is `(b, h, c)` - last prefix of each chunk. `F.pad(..., (1, 0))` adds 0 at the beginning, so `(b, h, c+1)`. `segsum` gives `(b, h, c+1, c+1)`. `decay_chunk[b,h,chunk_out,chunk_in] = exp(cumsum_last[chunk_out] - cumsum_last[chunk_in])` for chunk_in <= chunk_out. `new_states = torch.einsum("bhzc,bchpn->bzhpn", decay_chunk, states)` Wait, `bhzc` - b, h, z, c. z is output chunk, c is input chunk. `new_states[b,z,h,p,n] = sum_c decay_chunk[b,h,z,c] * states[b,c,h,p,n]` For c > z, decay_chunk is -inf -> 0. So `new_states[z] = sum_{c=0}^{z} exp(last_prefix[z] - last_prefix[c]) * states[c]`. Then `states = new_states[:, :-1]` - the last chunk is dropped? Actually `new_states[:, :-1]` takes all but the last chunk index. So now states is `(b, c, h, p, n)`. But note: `states[c]` before this step is `(initial_state + intra_state_0, intra_state_1, ..., intra_state_{c-1})`. After recurrence: `states_after[0] = decay_chunk[0,0] * initial + decay_chunk[0,1] * intra_0` Wait no, `decay_chunk` operates on c+1 elements where c ranges from 0 to num_chunks. `states` before einsum has indices 0..num_chunks (length num_chunks+1). Then `new_states[z] = sum_{c=0}^{z} exp(last_prefix[z] - last_prefix[c]) * states[c]`. Then taking `new_states[:, :-1]` gives z from 0 to num_chunks-1. For `c=2`: states[:,0] and states[:,1]. BUT WAIT. `new_states[:, :-1]` keeps ALL BUT THE LAST element. Elements: - index 0: new_states[:, 0] = initial (for chunk 0) - index 1: new_states[:, 1] = exp(lp0)*initial + intra_0 (for chunk 1) But what about `new_states[:, 2]`? So this is consistent. Then what the hell is going wrong? Let me reconsider `smem_inv_exp`. In my code: ```c if (tid == 0) { for (int i = 1; i < block_len; ++i) { smem_prefix[i] += smem_prefix[i-1]; } for (int i = 0; i < block_len; ++i) { float val = expf(smem_prefix[i]); smem_exp_prefix[i] = val; smem_inv_exp[i] = 1.0f / val; } } ``` Wait, `expf(smem_prefix[i])` for large prefix values can overflow to inf! And `smem_inv_exp[i] = 1.0f / inf = 0.0f`. And `smem_inv_exp[i] * smem_X[j,p]` becomes 0. But in the reference, the computation is: `L[i,j] = exp(prefix[i] - prefix[j])`. This is computed as `exp(prefix[i]) * exp(-prefix[j])` ONLY IF we're doing the math separately. But directly computing the difference is more numerically stable. For large positive prefixes, exp(prefix[i]) can overflow to inf, and exp(-prefix[j]) can underflow to 0. But their product exp(prefix[i]-prefix[j]) might be finite if prefix[i] ≈ prefix[j]. In my code: `y += dot * smem_inv_exp[j] * smem_X[j * d_head + p];` then `y *= smem_exp_prefix[i];` If `smem_exp_prefix[i]` is inf and `smem_inv_exp[j]` is 0, then `inf * 0 = NaN` in IEEE 754! AHHH! This is the bug! When prefix[i] is large positive, `exp(prefix[i])` overflows to inf. And `exp(-prefix[j])` underflows to 0 if prefix[j] is also large positive. Now, there's one more subtlety: using `expf` for potentially large values can cause overflow. In the reference, PyTorch computes `L = torch.exp(segsum(A_blocks))` where segsum computes `prefix[i] - prefix[j]`. For i=j, this is 0, so L[i,i]=1. For i > j, it could be large positive or negative. But PyTorch handles this with the same floating point limitations. My computation breaks it into `exp(prefix[i]) * exp(-prefix[j])`. If prefix[i] is large positive, exp(prefix[i]) overflows to inf, exp(-prefix[j]) underflows to 0. Their product could be NaN if both are inf and 0, OR it could be inf * normal if prefix[j] is also large. But actually, in the reference, `L[i,j] = exp(prefix[i] - prefix[j])`. If prefix[i] = 100 and prefix[j] = 99, then L = exp(1) ≈ 2.718. In my code: exp(100) ≈ inf, exp(-99) ≈ 1e-43. inf * 1e-43 = NaN (or maybe inf depending on the GPU). Wait, actually inf * 0 = NaN, but inf * small_nonzero could be inf. For exp(-99) ≈ 1.011e-43, which is subnormal but still non-zero until about exp(-126) for float32 normalized, or down to 2^-149 with subnormals. exp(100) is about 2.69e43, which is well above float32 max (3.4e38), so it overflows to inf. exp(-100) is about 3.7e-44, which is just barely above the smallest positive subnormal float32 (~1.4e-45). So it underflows to 0 or a subnormal. If exp(prefix[j]) underflows to 0, then exp(-prefix[j]) = 1/0 = inf. Wait, if prefix[j] = 100 and we compute exp(-100), that is exp(-100) ≈ 3.7e-44, which is > 0. 1/exp(100) ≈ 3.7e-44, so `smem_inv_exp[j]` = 3.7e-44, NOT inf. Actually the issue is: smem_exp_prefix[i] = exp(prefix[i]) → inf if prefix[i] > 88.7 smem_inv_exp[j] = 1/exp(prefix[j]) → if prefix[j] > 88.7, exp(prefix[j]) = inf, and 1/inf = 0. So smem_inv_exp[j] = 0. Then `smem_inv_exp[j] * smem_X[j,p]` = 0. And `y += dot * 0` = 0 (no NaN). Then `y *= smem_exp_prefix[i]` = `0 * inf` = NaN. AHA! So if prefix[i] overflows to inf and some prefix[j] also overflows (making inv_exp[j]=0), then we get NaN! But for prefix[j] > 88.7, both exp(prefix[i]) and exp(prefix[j]) overflow. For i > j, prefix[i] > prefix[j] (assuming all A are positive). Then: - exp(prefix[i]) = inf - exp(-prefix[j]) = 0 - product = NaN. In the reference, `exp(prefix[i] - prefix[j])` might be finite if the difference is small (e.g., prefix[i]=100, prefix[j]=99, difference=1, exp(1)=2.718). So my approach of computing exp(prefix) and exp(-prefix) separately is numerically unstable and can produce NaN! This is a CRITICAL issue. I need to compute `exp(prefix[i] - prefix[j])` directly, or use a more stable formulation. For Y_diag: `Y_diag[i,p] = sum_{j=0}^{i} dot(C[i], B[j]) * exp(prefix[i] - prefix[j]) * X[j,p]` I can rewrite: `Y_diag[i,p] = sum_{j=0}^{i} dot(C[i], B[j]) * exp(prefix[i] - prefix[j]) * X[j,p]` To avoid overflow/underflow, I can use the `exp` of the difference directly: `exp(prefix[i] - prefix[j])`. I can compute this on the fly as: ```c for (int j = 0; j <= i; ++j) { float dot = ...; y += dot * expf(smem_prefix[i] - smem_prefix[j]) * smem_X[j * d_head + p]; } ``` This is more expensive (computing exp for each pair), but numerically stable because the difference is small (sum of at most block_len=64 elements of A, which has std 1, so difference has std ~11.3, exp(11.3) ≈ 8e4, very safe). Similarly for the intra state: `intra[p,n] = sum_j X[j,p] * B[j,n] * exp(last_prefix - prefix[j])`. Here `last_prefix - prefix[j]` is the sum of A[j+1..L-1], which also has std ~sqrt(L) ~ 8. So exp is safe. For Y_off: `Y_off[i,p] = exp(prefix[i]) * sum_n C[i,n] * state[p,n]`. Here `prefix[i]` can be large (~N(0, 8)), and exp(prefix[i]) can overflow if prefix[i] > 88. But with mean 0 and std 8, P(prefix > 88) is ~ 10^{-19}, essentially impossible. Wait, prefix[i] is the prefix sum of i+1 random A values. For i=63 (end of chunk), prefix[63] = sum of 64 random A values. Mean = 0, std = 8. So prefix values are typically between -24 and 24 (3 sigma). exp(24) ≈ 2.6e10, well below float32 max. So `exp(prefix[i])` for i within a chunk is safe. `exp(last_prefix)` for the chunk is also safe. `exp(last_prefix - prefix[j])` is safe. The only issue was `exp(prefix[i]) * exp(-prefix[j])` when computed separately. So I should compute differences directly! For Y_diag: ```c float y = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j <= i; ++j) { float dot = 0.0f; for (int n = 0; n < d_state; ++n) { dot += ci[n] * smem_B[j * d_state + n]; } y += dot * expf(smem_prefix[i] - smem_prefix[j]) * smem_X[j * d_head + p]; } ``` Wait, but earlier I said the reference does Y_diag = exp(prefix[l]) * sum_j ... * exp(-prefix[j]). Both are equivalent mathematically, but the direct difference is numerically stable. For the intra state: ```c float val = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < block_len; ++j) { val += smem_X[j * d_head + p] * smem_B[j * d_state + n] * expf(A_last - smem_prefix[j]); } ``` This is direct difference and is safe. For Y_off: ```c float dot_state = 0.0f; for (int n = 0; n < d_state; ++n) { dot_state += ci[n] * smem_state[n * d_head + p]; } y += dot_state * expf(smem_prefix[i]); ``` Wait, but I was previously storing `smem_exp_prefix[i]` and `smem_inv_exp[i]` to avoid computing exp repeatedly. But since direct difference is numerically safer, I should compute exp on the fly for Y_diag. For Y_off, `exp(prefix[i])` is only computed block_len times per chunk, and then used for all d_head output elements. It's safe to compute it once and store in shared memory, since prefix[i] is typically small. So I can remove `smem_inv_exp` and just compute direct differences. I'll keep `smem_exp_prefix` for Y_off. Let me write the entire kernel from scratch to be sure. Actually, since I identified TWO issues (double decay and potential numerical overflow from separate exp computation), let me fix both. For Y_diag, computing `expf(smem_prefix[i] - smem_prefix[j])` for every i,j pair is O(L^2) per output element if done naively, but since we already loop over j for each i, it's just one extra exp per j. For L=64, each output element does 64 exps. For block_len*d_head=4096 output elements per block, that's 4096*64 = 262,144 exps per block. At 16K blocks, that's 4.3B exps. That might be expensive, but let's see if it's feasible. Alternatively, I can compute `exp_diff[i][j] = exp(prefix[i] - prefix[j])` once and store it in shared memory. For L=64, L*L = 4096 floats = 16KB shared memory. This is manageable! Kernel logic: ``` prev_last_prefix = 0.0f for each chunk: // Load A, B, C, X into smem // Compute prefix // For each output element (i, p): // ci = C[i, :] // y_diag = sum_{j=0}^i dot(ci, B[j]) * exp(prefix[i] - prefix[j]) * X[j,p] // y_off = exp(prefix[i]) * dot(ci, state[:, p]) // Y = y_diag + y_off // Compute intra = sum_j X[j,p] * B[j,n] * exp(last_prefix - prefix[j]) // inter_decay = exp(last_prefix - prev_last_prefix) // state = inter_decay * state + intra // prev_last_prefix = last_prefix ``` Wait, I still need to verify: does `state[:, p]` mean `state[n, p]` where n ranges over d_state? Actually, let me re-read the original reference code more carefully one more time. Maybe my understanding of the SSD algorithm is slightly off. In the reference: ```python # Rearrange into blocks/chunks X_blocks, A_blocks, B_blocks, C_blocks = [ rearrange(x, "b (c l) ... -> b c l ...", l=self.block_len) for x in (X, self.A, self.B, self.C) ] A_blocks = rearrange(A_blocks, "b c l h -> b h c l") ``` So A_blocks is `(b, h, c, l)`. ```python A_cumsum = torch.cumsum(A_blocks, dim=-1) ``` A_cumsum is `(b, h, c, l)`. ```python # 1. Compute diagonal block outputs L = torch.exp(self.segsum(A_blocks)) Y_diag = torch.einsum("bclhn,bcshn,bhcls,bcshp->bclhp", C_blocks, B_blocks, L, X_blocks) ``` Wait, `self.segsum(A_blocks)` where A_blocks is `(b, h, c, l)`. The `segsum` function does `torch.cumsum(x, dim=-1)` and then `x_cumsum[..., :, None] - x_cumsum[..., None, :]`. For A_blocks of shape `(b, h, c, l)`: - `x_cumsum` shape is `(b, h, c, l)`. - `x_cumsum[..., :, None]` shape is `(b, h, c, l, 1)`. - `x_cumsum[..., None, :]` shape is `(b, h, c, 1, l)`. - Subtraction gives `(b, h, c, l, l)`. - Then tril mask. So `L[b, h, c, l, s] = exp(cumsum[l] - cumsum[s])` for s <= l. This is the within-chunk causal mask. Correct. And the einsum: `Y_diag[b, c, l, h, p] = sum_{n,s} C[b, c, l, h, n] * B[b, c, s, h, n] * L[b, h, c, l, s] * X[b, c, s, h, p]`. This is exactly what I compute in `Y_diag`. Correct. ```python # 2. Let me also think about whether the reference's `segsum` implementation differs from my understanding. ```python def segsum(self, x): T = x.size(-1) x_cumsum = torch.cumsum(x, dim=-1) x_segsum = x_cumsum[..., :, None] - x_cumsum[..., None, :] mask = torch.tril(torch.ones(T, T, device=x.device, dtype=bool), diagonal=0) x_segsum = x_segsum.masked_fill(~mask, -torch.inf) return x_segsum ``` For input `x` of shape `(..., T)`: - `x_cumsum` is inclusive cumsum: `x_cumsum[t] = sum_{i=0}^t x[i]`. - `x_segsum[i, j] = x_cumsum[i] - x_cumsum[j]` for all i, j. - Then mask with tril(diagonal=0), so only positions where j <= i are kept. - Others are set to -inf. So `x_segsum[i, j] = x_cumsum[i] - x_cumsum[j]` for `j <= i`. For `A_blocks` of shape `(b, h, c, l)`: `L[b, h, c, i, j] = exp(x_cumsum[i] - x_cumsum[j])` for `j <= i`. Correct. ``` So the CORRECT decay for the state update is `exp(chunk_total)`, NOT `exp(chunk_total - prev_chunk_total)`. The compile passed. Let me send a correctness check for v3. Actually, let me carefully reconstruct the v3 code to make sure it's clean and then run it. In v3, I had: ```cpp float decay = expf(last_prefix); smem_state[idx] = decay * smem_state[idx] + smem_intra[idx]; ``` And `prev_last_prefix` was NOT used. The state update was simply multiplying by `exp(chunk_total)` and adding intra. And `chunk_total` is the prefix sum of the current chunk, which is `A_cumsum[b, h, c, -1]`. So `exp(chunk_total)` is `exp(A_total_c)`. Correct. And the state before chunk c is `smem_state` which was updated at the end of chunk c-1. For chunk 0, the state is `initial`. For chunk 1, the state is `exp(A0_total)*initial + intra_0`. So `decay = exp(A1_total)` and `new_state = exp(A1_total) * (exp(A0_total)*initial + intra_0) + intra_1`. This gives the correct state after chunk 1, which is also the state before chunk 2. Perfect. The v3 code should be correct. Let me run it. But I realize there might be another issue. In the v3 code, the `smem_A` was used for prefix sum but its initial values were loaded directly from A. After prefix sum, `smem_A[i]` became the prefix. This is fine. But then `smem_A` was reused for the next chunk. That's fine because we reload it. Let me compile a clean version and run correctness.
